Rejecting Unfounded Proofs and the Messenger Argument
Ghazali rejects two flawed proofs and establishes speech as necessary for messengership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali dismisses consensus and the Prophet's statement as independent proofs for divine speech, and how the attribute of speech is logically necessary for the concept of a messenger.

Core Idea
Without divine speech, the very idea of a messenger collapses.

Ghazali now addresses potential objections to his earlier proofs. Some might argue that divine speech can be established by consensus (ijma') or by the statement of the Prophet himself. He dismisses both as circular. He then deepens the argument by showing that denying God's speech makes the concept of a messenger impossible, thereby undermining the entire structure of revelation. This passage is a masterclass in logical coherence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the critique of consensus
Read the first sentence where Ghazali calls using consensus or the Prophet's statement a 'humiliating path'. Notice why each is insufficient: consensus depends on the Prophet's statement, and the Prophet's statement itself requires prior proof of divine speech.
Ask yourself: what makes this argument circular?
2
Trace the logical link between speech and messenger
In the second part, Ghazali defines a messenger as the conveyer of speech. Ask yourself: If God cannot speak, can there be a messenger? The analogy with the earth or mountain messenger illustrates the absurdity.
Notice how Ghazali uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument protects the integrity of prophethood and revelation. Without divine speech, the entire prophetic tradition would be incoherent.
This links divine attributes directly to prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أراد إثبات الكلام بالإجماع، أو بقول الرسول .. فقد سام نفسَهُ خطة خسف (٢) ؛ لأن الإجماع يستند إلى قول الرسول (٣) . والرسول ضعيف كذلك
ومن أنكر كونَ الباري تعالى متكلماً .. فبالضرورة ينكر تصور الرسول ؛ إذ معنى الرسولِ : المبلغ لكلام المرسل ؛ فإن لم يكن الكلام متصوراً في حق من ادعى أنه مرسل .. كيف يتصور الرسول ؟!
ومن قال : أنا رسولُ الأرض أو رسولُ الجبل إليكم .. فلا يصغى إليه ؛ لاعتقادنا استحالة الكلام والرسالة من الجبل والأرض ، ولله المثل الأعلى ; من اعتقد استحالة الكلام في حق الله تعالى.. استحالَ منه أن يصدق بالرسول ؛ إذ المكذِّبُ بالكلام لا بدَّ أن يكذِّبَ بتبليغ الكلام ؛ والرسالة المنهج المختار عبارة عن تبليغ الكلام ، والرسولُ عبارةٌ عن المبلغ
And whoever wants to prove speech (al-kalam) by consensus (ijma'), or by the statement of the Messenger... he has set himself upon a humiliating path (2); because consensus is based on the statement of the Messenger (3). And the Messenger is weak as well.
And whoever denies that the Exalted Creator is speaking... necessarily denies the conception of the messenger; for the meaning of 'messenger' is: the one who conveys the speech of the one who sent him; if speech is not conceivable in the case of the one who claims to be sent... how can a messenger be conceived?
And whoever says: 'I am the messenger of the earth or the messenger of the mountain to you'... he is not listened to, because we believe in the impossibility of speech and messengership from the mountain and the earth. And to Allah belongs the highest similitude; whoever believes in the impossibility of speech in the case of Allah the Exalted... it becomes impossible for him to believe in the messenger; for the denier of speech must necessarily deny the conveying of speech; and messengership, the chosen path, is the conveying of speech, and the messenger is the one who conveys.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents two connected arguments. First, Ghazali dismisses the use of ijma' (consensus) or the Prophet's statement as independent proofs for divine speech, calling it a 'humiliating path' (khatta khisf). The reason: consensus itself is based on the Prophet's statement, so it adds nothing; and the Prophet's statement itself depends on the attribute of speech being already proven, leading to circularity. Second, he argues that denying God's speech logically entails denying the very concept of a messenger (rasul). He defines a messenger as 'the conveyer of the speech of the sender' (al-muballigh li-kalam al-mursil). If speech is impossible for Allah, then a messenger cannot exist. He reinforces this with an analogy: if someone claimed to be a messenger of the earth or a mountain, we would reject it because such entities cannot speak. Similarly, one who denies divine speech cannot accept any messenger, which would undermine the entire edifice of revelation. Thus, the attribute of speech is essential to the concept of prophethood.

Key Terms
الإجماع
قول الرسول
خطة خسف
الرسول
تصور الرسول
المبلغ
استحالة الكلام
المثل الأعلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume consensus (ijma') is an independent proof for divine speech.
Correction: Ghazali shows consensus depends on the Prophet's statement, which itself relies on divine speech being already established, making the argument circular.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali consider using consensus or the Prophet's statement to prove divine speech a 'humiliating path'?
A) Because consensus is not a valid source of knowledge.
B) Because consensus depends on the Prophet's statement, and the Prophet's statement depends on the very attribute to be proven.
C) Because the Prophet never spoke about divine speech.
D) Because consensus is weaker than reason.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is not provable by appeals to consensus or prophetic authority alone; rather, the concept of a messenger logically necessitates that the sender can speak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God speaks, or have you reduced the Qur'an to a historical document? How does your conviction—or lack thereof—shape the way you receive the Prophet's message and act upon it? Commit this week to reading one verse as if God were speaking directly to you.
?
If you were to deny that God speaks, could you still intelligibly claim to follow a messenger? Ghazali says the very idea collapses. Have you ever treated divine speech as secondary or optional in your faith? Identify one practical area where you overlook God's speech (e.g., neglecting the Qur'an's commands) and decide to change.
?
Ghazali calls the attempt to prove speech through the Prophet's statement a 'humiliating path.' Are there areas in your own belief where you rely on circular arguments—things you accept simply because 'they are from the Prophet' without understanding their foundation? What would it take for you to ground your faith on the rock-solid necessity of God's speech?

Proving Divine Speech as Perfection
Ghazali proves divine speech by a tripartite logical elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third method for proving the attribute of divine speech through a logical tripartite classification.

Core Idea
Divine speech is a perfection, because it can only be perfection, deficiency, or neutral — and only perfection befits God.

Ghazali presents a concise logical argument to prove that Allah possesses the attribute of speech. He uses the same method he employed for hearing and sight: classifying speech among the three logical categories of perfection, deficiency, or neutral. By eliminating the latter two, he demonstrates that speech must be a perfection for any living being, and therefore necessarily attributed to the Perfect Being.

Guided Path
1
Understand the tripartite classification
Learn the three logical possibilities for speech: perfection, deficiency, or neutral. This is the starting point of Ghazali's argument.
Notice the method mirrors the proof for hearing and sight.
2
Eliminate the false possibilities
See why speech cannot be a deficiency or neutral — both are unbefitting of God and logically impossible for a perfect being.
Ghazali declares them 'false' (bāṭil) without further elaboration here, relying on prior principles.
3
Appreciate the conclusion
Conclude that divine speech is an essential perfection, part of God's attributes, and thus necessarily exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلعل الأقوم منهج لإثبات صفة الكلام ثالث؛ وهو الذي سلكناه في إثبات السمع والبصر في أن الكلام للحي: إما أن يقال: إنه كمالٌ، أو يقال: هو نقص، أو يقال: لا هو نقص ولا هو كمال، وباطل أن يقال: إنه نقص، أو لا هو نقص ولا هو كمال.. فثبت أنه كمال
Perhaps the soundest method for proving the attribute of speech (sifat al-kalam) is a third [method]; which is the one we followed in proving hearing and sight: that speech for the living being: either it is said that it is a perfection (kamal), or it is said that it is a deficiency (naqs), or it is said that it is neither deficiency nor perfection. And it is false to say that it is a deficiency, or that it is neither deficiency nor perfection. So it is established that it is a perfection.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the third and preferred method for proving that God possesses the attribute of speech (sifat al-kalam). Ghazali states that this is the same method he used earlier for proving the attributes of hearing and sight. The method is a tripartite logical classification: for any living being (al-hayy), speech can be either a perfection (kamal), a deficiency (naqs), or neither (la huwa naqs wa la huwa kamal). He then eliminates the latter two possibilities: it cannot be a deficiency because that would be unbefitting of God, and it cannot be neutral because that would be pointless and arbitrary. Therefore, it must be a perfection. Since God is perfect in every way, He must possess the perfection of speech. This argument relies on the principle that all perfections are necessarily attributable to God, while deficiencies are impossible for Him.

Key Terms
الأقوم منهج
صفة الكلام
السمع والبصر
الحي
كمال
نقص
باطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speech is a temporal or accidental attribute of God.
Correction: Ghazali argues it is an essential perfection belonging to God eternally.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's method, what are the logical possibilities for the speech of a living being?
A) Perfection only
B) Deficiency only
C) Perfection, deficiency, or neutral
D) Perfection or deficiency only

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves divine speech by showing that for any living being, speech must be a perfection, and God, being perfect, possesses it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If divine speech is a perfection, and your speech is meant to reflect that divine attribute, how often do your words actually fall short of that perfection? When was the last time you used speech as a deficiency rather than a perfection?
?
Consider a conversation you had recently where your words were either a perfection or a deficiency—no middle ground. Which was it? What does that reveal about your heart's alignment with the divine attribute of speech?
?
The proof eliminates the possibility that speech could be neutral. In your daily life, are there words you speak that you consider 'neutral' but that are actually either a deficiency or a perfection? How will you begin to see every word as an opportunity for kamāl?

Proving God's Speech as a Perfection via Priority
Ghazali enumerates a threefold division for speech and uses the priority principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali proves that God must possess the attribute of speech by classifying it as a perfection and applying the principle of priority.

Core Idea
Any perfection found in creatures necessarily belongs to the Creator by priority.

In this passage, Ghazali presents what he considers the most upright method for proving that God has the attribute of speech. He uses a logical division: speech for a living being is either a perfection, a deficiency, or neither. He eliminates the latter two, establishing speech as a perfection, and then applies the principle that every perfection of the creature belongs to the Creator by priority. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow register before interpretation.
Notice Ghazali refers to a previous method used for hearing and sight.
2
Understand the threefold division
Ghazali divides speech for a living being into three possibilities: perfection, deficiency, or neither. Grasp this classification.
3
See the elimination
Ghazali states it is false to say speech is a deficiency or neither. Therefore, it must be a perfection.
This is a logical elimination of false options.
4
Connect to the priority principle
Recall the principle used earlier: every perfection found in a creature is necessarily existent for the Creator by priority. Apply it here.
This principle transfers perfection from creature to Creator.
5
Reflect on the method
This is a rational proof independent of revelation. Consider its strength and limitations.
Ghazali will later address the objection that speech implies temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلعل الأقوم منهج لإثبات صفة الكلام ثالث؛ وهو الذي سلكناه في إثبات السمع والبصر في أن الكلام للحي: إما أن يقال: إنه كمالٌ، أو يقال: هو نقص، أو يقال: لا هو نقص ولا هو كمال، وباطل أن يقال: إنه نقص، أو لا هو نقص ولا هو كمال.. فثبت بالضرورة أنَّه كمال، وكلُّ كمال وُجد للمخلوق فهو واجب الوجود للخالق بطريق الأولى كما سبق
Perhaps the most upright method for proving the attribute of speech is a third one; it is the one we followed in proving hearing and sight: that speech for a living being is either said to be a perfection, or it is said to be a deficiency, or it is said to be neither deficiency nor perfection. And it is false to say it is a deficiency, or that it is neither deficiency nor perfection. So it is necessarily established that it is a perfection. And every perfection found in the creature is necessarily existent for the Creator by the method of priority (awlā), as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's preferred method for proving God's attribute of speech. It follows the pattern used for hearing and sight. The reasoning is a threefold division: speech for a living being is either a perfection, a deficiency, or neither. The two latter options are false because speech is clearly an excellence, not a flaw or indifferent. Therefore, it is a perfection. Then the principle of priority applies: any perfection found in a creature is even more necessarily present in the Creator. Hence, God must possess speech.

Key Terms
كمال (perfection)
نقص (deficiency)
بطريق الأولى (by priority)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God speaks, then His speech must be created or temporal like human speech.
Correction: Ghazali's argument here only establishes that speech is a perfection; he will later distinguish between created and uncreated speech to address temporality.

Checkpoint
What is the third method Ghazali uses to prove God's speech?
A) Consensus of scholars.
B) The Quranic proof that God spoke to Moses.
C) The division of speech into perfection, deficiency, or neither, then eliminating the latter two.
D) The argument from creation being commanded.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves God's speech by a logical division showing it is a perfection, then applying the principle that any perfection of the creature belongs to the Creator by priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the best human speech—wise counsel, comforting words, or eloquent guidance—do you truly believe that God's speech surpasses that infinitely? How does that shape your longing to hear His words in the Qur'an?
?
Is there any perfection in creation, such as mercy or justice, that you hesitate to attribute to God in its absolute form? What is holding you back from that conviction, and how does this passage challenge that hesitation?
?
If God's speech is a perfection and He speaks, what does that mean for how you listen to the Qur'an? Will you approach it as the speech of the Perfect One, demanding your full attention and reverence, or do you give it the same casual listening you give to human talk?

Objection and Answer: Distinguishing External and Internal Speech
Ghazali responds to the objection by distinguishing external speech from speech of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali distinguishes between external speech (sounds/letters) and internal speech (kalam nafsi) to defend the divine attribute of speech.

Core Idea
Speech of the soul is real and eternal, not composed of sounds or letters.

This passage presents a key objection against the possibility of God having speech, and Ghazali's innovative response. The objector argues that speech must be either sounds, power, or something unintelligible, all of which are impossible for God. Ghazali accepts the division but introduces the concept of kalam nafsi (speech of the soul) as a third kind that avoids the problems.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection first, noting how it divides the meaning of 'speech' into three possibilities.
The objector claims each possibility is impossible for God.
2
Identify the three divisions
The objection lists three meanings: sounds/letters, power to produce sounds, and a third unintelligible meaning. Understand why each is problematic.
Sounds are temporal and cannot subsist in God; power alone does not make one a speaker; a third meaning is unintelligible.
3
See Ghazali's initial response
Ghazali accepts the division as correct but denies only the impossibility of the third division.
This opens the door for his positive explanation.
4
Understand kalam nafsi
Ghazali introduces speech of the soul, which is neither sound nor letter, as a perfection that does not imply temporality or making God a locus of events.
This is the divine attribute of speech.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this distinction resolves the objection and preserves God's attribute of speech as eternal.
This is a central Ash'ari doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الكلام الذي جعلتموه منشأ نظركم هو كلامُ الخلق، وذلك إما أن يراد به الأصواتُ والحروف، أو يراد به القدرة على إيجاد الأصوات والحروف في نفس القادر، أو يراد به معنی ثالث سواهما • فإن أريد به الأصوات والحروف .. فهي حوادث، ومن الحوادث ما هي في حقنا ولكن لا يتصوَّرُ قيامها في ذات الله تعالى، وإن قام بغيره .. لم يكن هو متكلماً به، بل كان المتكلم المحل الذي قام به، وإن أريد به القدرة على خلق الأصوات.. فهو كمال، ولكن المتكلم ليس متكلماً باعتبار قدرته على خلق الأصوات فقط، بل باعتبار خلقه للكلام في نفسه، والله تعالى قادرٌ على خلق الأصوات، فله كمال القدرة، ولكن لا يكون متكلماً به إلا إذا خلق الصوت في نفسه، وهو محال؛ إذ يصير به محلاً للحوادث، فاستحال أن يكون متكلماً، وإن أريد بالكلام أمرٌ ثالث .. فليس بمفهوم، وإثبات ما لا يفهم محال . قلنا : هذا التقسيم صحيح، والسؤال في جميع أقسامه معترف به إلا في إنكار القسم الثالث، فإنا معترفون باستحالة قيام الأصوات بذاته وباستحالة كونه متكلماً بهذا الاعتبار، ولكنا نقول: الإنسانُ يُسمى متكلماً للكلام باعتبارين: أحدهما بالصوت والحرف، والآخر بكلام النفس الذي ليس بصوت ولا حرف، وذلك كمال، وهو في حق الله تعالى غير محال ولا هو دال على الحدوث، ونحنُ لا نثبتُ في حق الله تعالى إلا كلام النفس
For it is said: The speech which you made the basis of your consideration is the speech of creatures. That is either intended to mean sounds and letters, or intended to mean the power to produce sounds and letters in the agent himself, or intended to mean a third meaning besides them. If it is intended to mean sounds and letters, they are temporal events, and some temporal events are in our case but their subsisting in the essence of God is inconceivable; and if they subsist in something else, then He would not be speaking by them, but rather the speaker would be the locus in which they subsisted. If it is intended to mean the power to create sounds, it is a perfection, but the speaker is not a speaker merely by virtue of his power to create sounds, but by virtue of his creating speech in himself. And God is powerful over creating sounds, so He possesses the perfection of power, but He is not a speaker by it unless He creates the sound in Himself, and that is impossible because He would thereby become a locus for temporal events. Thus it is impossible for Him to be a speaker. And if speech is intended to mean a third thing, it is not understood, and establishing what is not understood is impossible. We say: This division is correct, and the difficulty in all its parts is admitted except in denying the third division. For we admit the impossibility of sounds subsisting in His essence and the impossibility of His being a speaker in that sense. But we say: A human is called a speaker for speech in two respects: one by sound and letter, and the other by the speech of the soul (kalam al-nafs) which is neither sound nor letter. And that is a perfection, and with respect to God Almighty it is not impossible, nor does it indicate temporality. And we only affirm for God Almighty the speech of the soul.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and Ghazali's answer. The objection divides the meaning of 'speech' into three possibilities: (1) sounds and letters, (2) the power to produce them, (3) a third meaning. The objector argues all are impossible for God: sounds are temporal and cannot subsist in God; power alone doesn't make one a speaker unless one produces sound in oneself, which would make God a locus of temporal events; and a third meaning is unintelligible. Ghazali responds by accepting the division but denying the impossibility of the third division. He introduces the concept of kalam nafsi (speech of the soul), which is not composed of sounds or letters. This kind of speech is a perfection and does not entail temporality or making God a locus of events. Thus, God's attribute of speech is understood as kalam nafsi, which is eternal and not subject to the objections raised against external speech.

Key Terms
كلام النفس
كلام لفظي
محل للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All speech must consist of sounds and letters.
Correction: Ghazali argues there is a kind of speech, kalam nafsi, which is internal and not composed of sounds or letters, and this applies to God's speech.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali answer the objection that speech as sounds or power is impossible for God?
A) He rejects the entire division as invalid.
B) He accepts the division but affirms a third kind of speech called kalam nafsi.
C) He argues that God's speech is metaphorical.
D) He claims sounds can subsist in God's essence.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends God's speech as an eternal attribute by distinguishing it from created sounds and letters, affirming the reality of kalam nafsi.

Are you willing to accept that God's primary speech is silent and internal, or do you demand signs that suit your physical senses? What does your preference reveal about your trust in His transcendence?

How might shifting your focus change the way you approach recitation and reflection? Commit to one concrete change in your recitation practice this week to connect with the kalam nafsi.
?
Ghazali defends God's speech as an eternal attribute, not created. Do you truly live as if the Qur'an is the eternal, uncreated word of God, or do you treat it as a human book? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that the Qur'an is the very speech of God's essence, not a created thing? Divine Attributes

The Objection on Divine Speech and the Response with Inner Speech
Ghazali presents an objection against the proof of divine speech from perfection and answers by distinguishing kalam al-nafs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's defense of divine speech as inner speech (kalam al-nafs) against the objection that speech cannot be attributed to God.

Core Idea
Divine speech is not sound or letter but an eternal attribute: inner speech.

Ghazali has argued that speech is a perfection found in creation and must belong to the Creator. But an objector raises a critical challenge: what do we mean by speech? If it is sounds and letters, they are accidents incompatible with God. If it is the power to create sounds, that does not make God a speaker. Ghazali responds by introducing a third meaning of speech—inner speech—which is a non-physical attribute and is perfectly attributable to God. In this lesson, we examine this objection and Ghazali's powerful rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objector's challenge
Read the objection where the objector divides speech into three possibilities and argues each fails to apply to God.
Notice the objector's starting point: speech is a perfection, but only as found in creation.
2
Identify the three divisions
The objector lists three possible meanings: sounds/letters, power to create sounds, and an unknown third meaning. He argues against each.
The third meaning is initially dismissed as unintelligible.
3
See Ghazali's acceptance and critique
Ghazali agrees with the division but criticizes the denial of the third meaning, affirming it as inner speech.
He concedes that sounds cannot subsist in God and that power alone does not make one a speaker.
4
Grasp the evidence for inner speech
Ghazali provides common human experience and poetry as proof that inner speech is a distinct reality beyond sound and power.
Examples: 'I prepared speech in myself' and the poet's verse about speech in the heart.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Inner speech is a perfection possible for God, does not imply temporality, and is the true meaning of divine speech.
Thus, the objection fails because it ignored a valid meaning of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالضرورة أنَّه كمال ، وكلُّ كمال وُجد للمخلوق فهو واجب الوجود للخالق بطريق الأولى كما سبق . فإن قيل : الكلام الذي جعلتموه منشأ نظركم هو كلامُ الخلق ، وذلك إما [؟] إثبات الكلام بهذا المسلك أن يراد به الأصواتُ والحروف ، أو يراد به القدرة على إيجاد الأصوات [؟] والحروف في نفس القادر ، أو يراد به معنی ثالث سواهما . فإن أريد به الأصوات والحروف فهي حوادث ، ومن الحوادث ما هي في حقنا ولكن لا يتصوَّرُ قيامها في ذات الله تعالى ، وإن قام بغيره لم يكن هو متكلماً به ، بل كان المتكلم المحل الذي قام به ، وإن أريد به القدرة على خلق الأصوات فهو كمال ، ولكن المتكلم ليس متكلماً باعتبار قدرته على خلق الأصوات فقط ، بل باعتبار خلقه للكلام في نفسه ، والله تعالى قادرٌ على خلق الأصوات ، فله كمال القدرة ، ولكن لا يكون متكلماً به إلا إذا خلق الصوت في نفسه ، وهو محال ؛ إذ يصير به محلاً للحوادث ، فاستحال أن يكون متكلماً ، وإن أريد بالكلام أمرٌ ثالث فليس بمفهوم ، وإثبات ما لا يفهم محال . قلنا : هذا التقسيم صحيح ، والسؤال في جميع أقسامه معترف به إلا في إنكار القسم الثالث ، فإنا معترفون باستحالة قيام الأصوات بذاته وباستحالة كونه متكلماً بهذا الاعتبار ، ولكنا نقول : الإنسانُ يُسمى متكلماً باعتبارين : أحدهما بالصوت والحرف ، والآخر بكلام النفس الذي ليس بصوت ولا حرف ، وذلك كمال ، وهو في حق الله تعالى غير محال ولا هو دال على الحدوث ، ونحنُ لا نثبتُ في حق الله تعالى إلا كلام النفس ، وكلام النفس لا سبيل إلى إنكاره في حق الإنسان زائداً على القدرة والصوت ، حتى يقول الإنسان : زوَّرْتُ البارحة كلاماً في نفسي ، ويقال : في نفس فلان كلام وهو يريد أن ينطق به ، ويقول الشاعر : لا يُعْجِبَنَّكَ مِنْ أَثِيرِ خَطُهُ حَتَّى يَكُونَ مَعَ الْكَلَامِ أَصِيلا إنَّ الكَلامَ لَفِي الْفُؤادِ وَإِنَّمَا جُعِلَ اللِّسَانُ عَلَى الْفُؤادِ دَلِيلا وما ينطق به الشعراء يدلُّ على أنه من الجليات التي يشترك كافة الخلق في دركها ، فكيف ينكر ؟
By necessity it is a perfection, and every perfection found in the created is necessarily existent for the Creator in a primary way, as previously. If it is said: The speech which you made the origin of your consideration is the speech of creation, and that is either [?] establishing speech through this path: that by it is meant sounds and letters, or by it is meant the power to bring into existence sounds [?] and letters in the essence of the powerful one, or by it is meant a third meaning besides them. If by it is meant sounds and letters, then they are accidents, and among accidents are those which are in our right but their subsistence in the essence of God Most High is inconceivable; and if they subsist in other than Him, then He is not speaking by them, but rather the speaker would be the locus in which they subsist. If by it is meant the power to create sounds, then it is a perfection, but the speaker is not speaking by virtue of his power to create sounds only, but by virtue of his creation of speech in himself. God Most High is powerful over creating sounds, so He possesses the perfection of power, but He is not speaking by it unless He creates the sound in Himself, and that is impossible because He would thereby become a locus for accidents, so it is impossible that He be speaking. If by speech is meant a third thing, then it is not understood, and establishing what is not understood is impossible. We say: This division is correct, and the question in all its parts is acknowledged except in the denial of the third part. For we acknowledge the impossibility of sounds subsisting in His essence and the impossibility of His being speaking in this respect. But we say: Man is called speaking in two respects: one by sound and letter, and the other by inner speech (kalam al-nafs) which is neither sound nor letter. That is a perfection, and it is not impossible in the right of God Most High nor does it indicate temporality. We only establish in the right of God Most High inner speech. And inner speech cannot be denied in the right of man, beyond power and sound, to the point that man says: 'I prepared speech in myself last night', and it is said: 'In so-and-so's soul is speech while he wants to utter it.' And the poet says: 'Do not admire from a scribe his handwriting until he becomes, with speech, genuine. Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue was made only a guide to the heart.' And what the poets utter indicates that it is among the self-evident matters in which all creation share in perceiving. So how can it be denied?
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. It begins by stating the premise from a previous proof: that speech is a perfection, and every perfection found in creation must exist in the Creator in a superior manner. Then an objector raises a problem: if we consider created speech (sounds, letters, or power to create them), it cannot be attributed to God. The objector divides the meaning of 'speech' into three possibilities: (1) sounds and letters, (2) the power to create sounds and letters, or (3) a third meaning beyond these. He then argues against each: sounds and letters are accidents, and accidents cannot subsist in God's essence; if they subsist in another, then that other would be the speaker, not God. The power to create sounds is a perfection, but it does not make God a speaker unless He creates sound in Himself, which is impossible because it would make Him a locus of accidents. A third meaning, if not understood, cannot be affirmed. Thus, no form of speech seems attributable to God. In response, Ghazali accepts the threefold division but criticizes the objector for denying the third part. He affirms that the third meaning is indeed understood: it is inner speech (kalam al-nafs), which is not sound or letter. He argues that humans are called speakers in two ways: by uttered speech (sound and letter) and by inner speech, a distinct attribute beyond power and sound. This inner speech is a perfection, and it is neither impossible for God nor does it imply temporality. Ghazali then provides evidence: humans commonly say 'I prepared speech in myself' or 'he has speech in his soul,' and poets express that speech resides in the heart. These are self-evident matters known to all people. Therefore, the third meaning is valid, and divine speech can be affirmed as inner speech without the problems of accidents.

كلام
حادث
قدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine speech must be composed of sounds and letters like human speech.
Correction: Ghazali shows that speech can also be inner speech (kalam al-nafs), which is not sound or letter, and thus can be attributed to God without implying temporality.

Checkpoint
How many possible meanings of 'kalam' does the objector propose, and which one does Ghazali affirm?
A) Two: sound and power; Ghazali affirms power.
B) Three: sound, power, and a third meaning; Ghazali affirms the third as inner speech.
C) Two: inner and outer speech; Ghazali affirms outer speech.
D) Three: letters, words, and sentences; Ghazali affirms all three.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends divine speech as inner speech (kalam al-nafs), a non-physical attribute that avoids the problems of accidents and temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have now learned that God's speech is an eternal, non-physical attribute. When you recite the Quran, do you truly feel you are encountering the very words of the Eternal, or have your recitations become routine? How would your heart transform if you believed each verse is His timeless inner speech addressing you now?
?
Ghazali says even humans have inner speech, evident when we form thoughts before speaking. When you talk to God in supplication (du'a), are your words merely sounds, or do they arise from the depths of your inner self? Will you today try to pray with the awareness that your inner speech is being heard by the One whose inner speech created the universe?
?
The poet says, 'Speech is in the heart, and the tongue is its guide.' If God's eternal speech is in His essence, then His commands (like 'Be') have always existed. You exist because of that command. Do you live as if you were called into being by an eternal voice? What is one area of your life where you can better obey that eternal command starting this hour?

How Moses Heard the Eternal Speech
Ghazali addresses the objection of how Moses heard Allah's eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's solution to the apparent paradox of hearing an eternal, non-physical speech.

Core Idea
Hearing Allah’s speech is direct perception of His eternal attribute.

Ghazali addresses a common objection: if Allah's speech is an eternal attribute without letters or sounds, how did Moses hear it? The objector argues that hearing requires sounds or letters. Ghazali clarifies that Moses perceived the divine attribute directly, not through created sounds. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Note how Ghazali sets up the objection and his answer.
2
Identify the objection's structure
The objector presents a dilemma: either Moses heard sounds/letters (which would mean he heard creation, not God's speech) or he heard nothing. Find the exact wording.
3
Examine Ghazali's answer
Ghazali's answer is concise: Moses heard the eternal attribute. Reflect on how this resolves the dilemma.
4
Consider the implication
Think about what this means for the nature of divine speech and prophetic experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان كلام النفس دقيقاً زلَّ عن ذهن أكثر الضعفاء ، فلم يثبتوا إلا حروفاً وأصواتاً .. توجه لهم على هذا المذهب أسئلة واستبعادات نشير إلى تبة [؟] ترد على هذه بعضها ليستدل بها على طريق الدفع في غيرها : الأول : الصفة يجب دفعها قول القائل : كيف سمع موسى عليه السلام كلام الله تعالى ؟ أسمع صوتاً تحريجة: كيف سمع عليه السلام وحرفاً ؟ فإن قلتم ذلك .. فإذن لم يسمع كلام الله تعالى ، فَإِنَّ كلام الله تعالى كلامه سبحانه وهو ليس بحروف ، وإن لم يسمع حرفاً ولا صوتاً .. فكيف يسمع ما ليس بحرف ولا صوت ؟ قلنا : سمع كلام الله تعالى ، وهو صفةٌ قديمة قائمة بذات الله تعالى ، والتي هي ليست بحرف ولا صوت؟
And since internal speech is subtle, it slipped from the minds of most of the weak, so they affirmed only letters and sounds. Questions and improbabilities concerning this doctrine confront them. We will point to some of these that occur (?) so that one may be guided by them to the way of repelling others. The first: (the attribute must be repelled?) The statement of the one who says: How did Moses, peace be upon him, hear the speech of God, exalted be He? Did he hear a sound? An objection: How did he hear? A letter? If you say that, then he did not hear the speech of God, for the speech of God is His speech, and it is not letters. And if he did not hear a letter or a sound, then how does he hear what is not a letter or a sound? We said: He heard the speech of God, and it is an eternal attribute subsisting in the essence of God, exalted be He, which is not a letter or a sound.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding how Moses heard Allah's speech, given that kalam is internal and not letters or sounds. Ghazali notes that because internal speech is subtle, many people only recognize external speech (letters and sounds), leading them to ask: Did Moses hear a sound? A letter? If so, then he did not hear Allah's speech since it is not letters or sounds. If not, how can one hear something that is not a letter or sound? Ghazali answers simply: Moses heard Allah's speech, which is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, and this attribute is not a letter or sound. The implication is that hearing can be direct perception of the attribute, not necessarily through sound waves. This guards against the misunderstanding that divine speech must be created sounds.

Misconception: Hearing Allah's speech must involve hearing sounds or letters.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that Moses directly perceived the eternal attribute of speech, which is not composed of sounds or letters.

Key Takeaway
Moses heard Allah's eternal attribute of speech directly, not through created sounds or letters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The lesson says internal speech is subtle and most people miss it. In your own life, are you content with the surface of religious practice (like mere recitation) without striving to perceive the deeper reality of Allah's attributes? What step will you take today to move beyond the letters?

How Did Moses Hear God's Speech? Ghazali's Analogy of Taste
Ghazali answers how Moses heard God's speech using the analogy of tasting sweetness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the question 'how' about hearing God's speech to be invalid and how he uses the analogy of tasting sweetness to explain it.

Core Idea
God's speech cannot be understood through the senses; only direct experience or analogy can hint at it.

Ghazali tackles a common objection: if divine speech is not letters or sounds, how did Moses hear it? He argues that the question itself is flawed because it demands a sensory modality for something beyond sensory perception. Using the analogy of tasting sugar, he shows that some things are only known by direct experience or imperfect comparison.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: if Moses heard sounds/letters, he didn't hear God's speech; if no sound/letter, how can he hear? Recognize the dilemma.
The objector assumes hearing must involve letters/sounds.
2
Grasp Ghazali's initial response
Ghazali says the question 'how' is invalid for a unique attribute. He calls it the speech of one who does not understand the import of 'how'.
The questioner is like someone asking about an unexperienced sensation.
3
Examine the analogy of sugar and honey
Ghazali compares hearing God's speech to perceiving sweetness. Two answers: give sugar to taste (direct experience) or analogize to honey (imperfect).
Direct experience is best; analogy works if similar, but fails if no common ground.
4
Apply the analogy to hearing
Direct experience is impossible for us (only Moses). Any analogy is imperfect because divine speech is unique. Thus, the question cannot be fully answered.
This shows the limits of human inquiry into divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان كلام النفس دقيقاً زلَّ عن ذهن أكثر الضعفاء ، فلم يثبتوا إلا حروفاً وأصواتاً .. توجه لهم على هذا المذهب أسئلة واستبعادات نشير إلى تبة ترد على هذه بعضها ليستدل بها على طريق الدفع في غيرها :
الأول :
الصفة يجب دفعها
قول القائل : كيف سمع موسى عليه السلام كلام الله تعالى ؟ أسمع صوتاً و[أم] حرفاً ؟ فإن قلتم ذلك .. فإذن لم يسمع كلام الله تعالى ، فَإِنَّ كلام الله تعالى كلامه سبحانه وهو ليس بحروف ، وإن لم يسمع حرفاً ولا صوتاً .. فكيف يسمع ما ليس بحرف ولا صوت ؟
قلنا : سمع كلام الله تعالى ، وهو صفةٌ قديمة قائمة بذات الله تعالى ، ليس بحرف ولا صوت ، فقولكم : كيف سمعَ كلام الله تعالى .. كلامُ مَنْ لا يفهمُ المطلب من سؤال ( كيف ) ، فإنَّه ماذا يطلب به ، وبماذا يمكن جوابه ؟ فليفهم ذلك حتى يُعرف استحالة السؤال
فنقول : السمع نوع إدراك ، فقول القائل : كيف سمع . . كقول القائل : كيف أدركت بحاسة الذوق حلاوة السكر ؟ وهذا السؤال لا سبيل إلى شفائه إلا بوجهين :
أحدهما : أن نسلّمَ سكراً إلى هذا السائل حتى يذوقه ويدرك طعمه وحلاوته ، فتقول : أدركتُه أنا كما أدركته أنت الآن ، وهذا هو الجواب الشافي والتعريف النائم [؟].
والثاني : أن يتعذر ذلك ؛ إما لفقد السكر ، أو لعدم الذوق في السائل للسكر ، فتقولُ : أدركتُ طعمه كما أدركت أنت حلاوة العسل ، فيكون هذا جواباً صواباً من وجه وخطاً من وجه ؛ أما وجه كونه صواباً : فإنه تعريف بشيء يشبه المسؤول عنه من وجه وإن كان لا يشبهه من كل الوجوه ؛ فإنَّ طعم العسل يخالف طعم السكر وإن قارَبَهُ من بعض الوجوه وهو أصل الحلاوة ، وهذا غاية الممكن ، فإن لم يكن السائل قد ذاق حلاوة شيء أصلاً .. تعذَّرَ جوابُهُ وتفهيم ما سأل عنه ، وكان كالعنين يَسأَلُ الجماع وقط ما أدركَهُ ؛ فيمتنع تفهيمه الحالة التي يدركها المجامع إلا أن يشبهه بلذَّةِ الأكل ، فيكون خطاً من وجه ؛ إذ للَّه الجماع والحالة التي يدركها المجامع لا تساوي الحالة التي يدركُها الآكل إلا من حيثُ إِنَّ عموم اسم اللذة قد يشملها ، فإن لم يكن قد التذَّ بشيء قطُّ ؟.. تعدَّرَ أَصلُ الجواب عن لذَّة
وكذلك من قال : كيف يُسمعُ كلام الله تعالى .. فلا يمكن شفاؤه من السؤال إلا بأن نُسمعَهُ كلام الله تعالى القديم، وهو متعذِّرٌ ؛ فإن ذلك من خصائص الكليم عليه السلام ، فنحن لا نقدرُ على إسماعه ، أو نُشبه ذلك
And since inner speech (kalam al-nafs) is subtle and slips from the minds of most weak people, so they only affirm letters and sounds. There arose against this school questions and improbabilities; we point to some of them that occur, so that one may learn from them the method of replying to others. First: the attribute must be repelled[?]. The objector says: How did Moses (peace be upon him) hear the speech of God Most High? Did he hear a sound or a letter? If you say that, then he did not hear the speech of God Most High, for the speech of God Most High is His speech, exalted be He, and it is not letters. If he did not hear a letter or a sound, then how does one hear what is not a letter nor a sound? We say: He heard the speech of God Most High, which is an eternal attribute subsisting in the essence of God Most High, not a letter nor a sound. So your saying 'how did he hear the speech of God Most High' is the speech of one who does not understand the import of the question 'how' – for what does it seek, and with what can it be answered? Let him understand that, so that the impossibility of the question may be known. Then we say: Hearing is a type of perception. So the saying of one who says 'how did he hear' is like the saying of one who says 'how did you perceive the sweetness of sugar with the sense of taste?' This question cannot be cured except in two ways: One: that we hand over sugar to this questioner so that he tastes it and perceives its taste and sweetness, and you say 'I perceived it as you now perceive it' – and this is the complete answer and the clear definition[?]. The second: that this be impossible, either due to lack of sugar or lack of taste in the questioner for sugar, so you say 'I perceived its taste as you perceive the sweetness of honey' – and this is an answer correct in one respect and wrong in another. As for the respect in which it is correct: it is a definition by something similar to the thing asked about in one respect, even if not similar in all respects; for the taste of honey differs from the taste of sugar, even if it approximates it in some respects, namely the essence of sweetness. This is the utmost possible. If the questioner has never tasted any sweetness at all, his answer and understanding of what he asked about become impossible, and he becomes like a eunuch who asks about intercourse, having never experienced it; it is impossible to make him understand the state perceived by the one engaging in intercourse unless one analogizes it to the pleasure of eating – which is wrong in one respect, for the pleasure of intercourse and the state perceived by the one engaging in intercourse are not equal to the state perceived by the eater except insofar as the general name 'pleasure' may include them. If he has never experienced any pleasure at all, then the basis of answering about pleasure becomes impossible. Likewise, he who says 'how is the speech of God Most High heard?' – his question cannot be cured except by making him hear the eternal speech of God Most High, which is impossible, for that is among the special characteristics of Moses, the interlocutor (peace be upon him), so we are not able to make him hear it, or by analogizing it.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (su'al wa jawab). Ghazali first notes that the subtlety of kalam al-nafs causes the weak to deny it and ask objections. The first objection asks how Moses (AS) heard God's speech: if he heard sounds/letters, then he did not hear God's speech (which is not letters); if he heard nothing, how can he hear something that is not sound? Ghazali's answer has two parts: first, he shows that the question 'how' is misguided because it seeks a modality that cannot be described for a unique, unexperienced attribute. He compares it to asking how one perceives the sweetness of sugar: the only adequate answer is to let the questioner taste it. If that is impossible, one must analogize to something similar (like honey). If the questioner has no experience of sweetness at all, explanation is impossible. Similarly, the hearing of God's speech is unique to Moses; we cannot make anyone experience it, and any analogy will be imperfect. The passage demonstrates the Ash'ari position that divine speech is an eternal attribute, not composed of letters and sounds, and that the mode of its perception is beyond our comprehension and can only be hinted at by analogy.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's speech must be composed of letters and sounds because that's how humans hear.
Correction: Ghazali argues that God's speech is an eternal attribute, not made of letters/sounds. The mode of hearing it is beyond normal sensory perception and can only be hinted at by analogy.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how can the question of how Moses heard God's speech be answered?
A) By explaining that he heard letters and sounds.
B) By saying that hearing is a type of perception and using the analogy of tasting sugar.
C) By stating that the question is invalid and no answer is possible.
D) By arguing that Moses did not actually hear anything.

Key Takeaway
The only way to understand how Moses heard God's speech is to recognize it as a unique perception that cannot be explained by sensory categories; direct experience or imperfect analogy are the only options.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever demanded God to explain Himself in a way that fits your limited experience? What does Ghazali's analogy of the eunuch asking about intercourse reveal about your own spiritual arrogance?
?
When you read verses like 'And Moses fell down unconscious' after hearing God's speech, do you secretly reduce it to a mere auditory event? How does that betray a lack of trust in the reality of divine attributes beyond your senses?
?
If you were to stand before God and hear His speech, would you be satisfied only if it matched your expectation of sounds and letters? What does the impossibility of describing that experience tell you about the limits of your intellectual pride?

The Impossibility of Asking 'How' About Allah's Speech
Ghazali refutes the question 'how is Allah's speech heard?' using sensory analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why asking 'how' about Allah's speech is fundamentally impossible and what this teaches us about divine attributes.

Core Idea
Asking 'how' about God is like asking a deaf person to describe sound.

This lesson examines a key argument from Imam al-Ghazali regarding the question of how Allah's speech is heard. He demonstrates that such a question is inherently flawed because it seeks to liken the divine to the created. Through powerful analogies, he shows that the inability to describe the modality of an attribute does not negate its existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of Ghazali's argument. Notice the flow from the sugar analogy to the eunuch and deaf examples.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the two ways to answer 'how'
Ghazali says a 'how' question about taste can be answered by direct experience or by analogy. Identify why direct experience is impossible for Allah's speech.
Direct experience of Allah's speech was only for Prophet Moses.
3
Examine the eunuch and deaf analogies
These examples show that someone who has never experienced something cannot be made to understand it through analogy alone. Consider how this applies to the question about Allah's speech.
The eunuch cannot truly understand intercourse; the deaf cannot understand sound.
4
Apply the argument to divine attributes
Ghazali concludes that asking 'how' about hearing Allah's speech or seeing Allah in the afterlife is impossible because these attributes have no likeness. This does not mean they don't exist; it means we must affirm them without modality.
This preserves tanzih (transcendence) while affirming the attributes.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the inability to describe a divine attribute does not negate it. We believe in Allah's speech as an eternal attribute, heard in a way befitting His majesty.
This lesson guards against both anthropomorphism and denial of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس بحرف ولا صوت، فقولكم: كيف سمعَ كلام الله تعالى.. كلامُ مَنْ لا يفهمُ المطلب من سؤال (كيف)، فإنَّه ماذا يطلب به، وبماذا يمكن جوابه؟ فليفهم ذلك حتى يُعرف استحالة السؤال. فنقول: السمع نوع إدراك، فقول القائل: كيف سمع.. كقول القائل: كيف أدركت بحاسة الذوق حلاوة السكر؟ وهذا السؤال لا سبيل إلى شفائه إلا بوجهين: أحدهما: أن نسلّمَ سكراً إلى هذا السائل حتى يذوقه ويدرك طعمه وحلاوته، فتقول: أدركتُه أنا كما أدركته أنت الآن، وهذا هو الجواب الشافي والتعريف النائم. والثاني: أن يتعذر ذلك؛ إما لفقد السكر، أو لعدم الذوق في السائل للسكر، فتقولُ: أدركتُ طعمه كما أدركت أنت حلاوة العسل، فيكون هذا جواباً صواباً من وجه وخطاً من وجه؛ أما وجه كونه صواباً: فإنه تعريف بشيء يشبه المسؤول عنه من وجه وإن كان لا يشبهه من كل الوجوه؛ فإنَّ طعم العسل يخالف طعم السكر وإن قارَبَهُ من بعض الوجوه وهو أصل الحلاوة، وهذا غاية الممكن، فإن لم يكن السائل قد ذاق حلاوة شيء أصلاً.. تعذَّرَ جوابُهُ وتفهيم ما سأل عنه، وكان كالعنين يَسأَلُ الجماع وقط ما أدركَهُ؛ فيمتنع تفهيمه الحالة التي يدركها المجامع إلا أن يشبهه بلذَّةِ الأكل، فيكون خطاً من وجه؛ إذ للَّه الجماع والحالة التي يدركها المجامع لا تساوي الحالة التي يدركُها الآكل إلا من حيثُ إِنَّ عموم اسم اللذة قد يشملها، فإن لم يكن قد التذَّ بشيء قطُّ؟.. تعذَّرَ أصل الجواب عن لذَّة. وكذلك من قال: كيف يُسمعُ كلام الله تعالى.. فلا يمكن شفاؤه من السؤال إلا بأن نُسمعَهُ كلام الله تعالى القديم، وهو متعذِّرٌ؛ فإن ذلك من خصائص الكليم عليه السلام، فنحن لا نقدرُ على إسماعه، أو نُشبه ذلك بشيء من مسموعاته، وليس في مسموعاته ما يشبه كلام الله تعالى؛ فَإِنَّ كلَّ مسموعاته التي ألفها أصوات، والأصواتُ لا تشبه ما ليس بأصوات، فيتعدَّرُ تفهيمه، بل الأصم لو سأل وقال: كيف تسمعون أنتم الأصوات وهو ما سمع صوتاً قط؟.. لم نقدر على جوابه؛ فإنَّا إن قلنا: كما تدرك أنت المبصراتِ، فهو إدراك في الأذن كإدراك البصر في العين.. كان هذا خطاً؛ فإنَّ إدراك الأصوات لا يشبه إبصارَ الألوان؛ فدلَّ أنَّ هذا السؤال محال السؤال فاسد من اصله؛ لأنه سؤال عن بل لو قال القائل: كيف يُرى ربُّ الأرباب في الآخرة.. كان جوابه محالاً؛ لأنه يسأل عن كيفية ما لا كيفية له؛ إذ معنى قول القائل: كيف هو؛ أي: مثل أي شيء هو مما عرفناه؟ فإن كان ما يسأل عنه غير مماثل لشيء مما عرفه.. كان الجواب محالاً، ولم يدل ذلك على عدم ذات الله تعالى فكذلك تعذُّرُ هذا لا يدلُّ على عدم كلام الله تعالى، بل ينبغي أن يعتقد أنَّ كلامه صفةٌ قديمة ليس كمثلها شيء؛ كما أن ذاته ذات قديمة ليس كمثلها شيء، وكما تُرى ذاته رؤية تخالف رؤية الأجسام والأعراض ولا تشبهها.. فيسمع كلامه تعالى سماعاً يخالف الحروف والأصوات ولا يشبهها.
It is not a letter nor a sound. So your saying: 'How is the speech of Allah heard?' is the speech of one who does not understand the demand of the question 'how'? For what does he seek by it, and with what can it be answered? Let him understand that so that the impossibility of the question is known. We say: hearing is a type of perception. So the saying of the questioner: 'How did you hear?' is like the saying of the questioner: 'How did you perceive the sweetness of sugar by the sense of taste?' This question has no way to be cured except by two ways: One: that we hand over sugar to this questioner until he tastes it and perceives its taste and sweetness, then you say: 'I perceived it as you have now perceived it.' This is the satisfying answer and the complete definition. The second: that this is impossible, either due to lack of sugar or lack of taste for sugar in the questioner, then you say: 'I perceived its taste as you perceived the sweetness of honey.' This is an answer correct in one respect and wrong in another; as for the aspect of its being correct: it is a definition by something that resembles the thing asked about in one respect, even if it does not resemble it in all respects. For the taste of honey differs from the taste of sugar, even if it approximates it in some respects, namely the principle of sweetness. And this is the utmost possible. If the questioner has never tasted any sweetness at all, then answering him and making him understand what he asked about becomes impossible, and he is like a eunuch who asks about sexual intercourse and has never experienced it; it is impossible to make him understand the state that the one engaging in intercourse perceives, unless by likening it to the pleasure of eating, which is wrong in one respect; for the pleasure of intercourse and the state perceived by the one engaging in intercourse are not equal to the state perceived by the eater except insofar as the general name of pleasure may encompass both. If he has never taken pleasure in anything at all, then the principle of answering about pleasure becomes impossible. And likewise, whoever says: 'How is the speech of Allah heard?' cannot be cured of the question except by making him hear the eternal speech of Allah, which is impossible; for that is among the characteristics of the intimate one (Kalim), peace be upon him; we are not able to make him hear it, or we liken it to something among his heard things, and among his heard things there is nothing resembling the speech of Allah. For all the heard things he is accustomed to are sounds, and sounds do not resemble what is not sounds. So making him understand becomes impossible. Rather, if a deaf person asked and said: 'How do you hear sounds?' while he has never heard a sound at all, we would not be able to answer him. For if we say: 'Just as you perceive visible things, and it is perception in the ear like the perception of the eye of sight,' this would be wrong; for the perception of sounds does not resemble the seeing of colors. So this indicates that this question is impossible, the question is corrupt in its origin; because it is a question about—rather, if someone said: 'How is the Lord of lords seen in the afterlife?' his answer would be impossible; because he asks about the modality of that which has no modality. For the meaning of the saying of the questioner: 'How is He?' is: 'Like which thing among what we know is He?' If that which is asked about is not similar to anything he knows, then the answer is impossible. And that does not indicate the non-existence of the essence of Allah; likewise, the impossibility of this does not indicate the non-existence of the speech of Allah. Rather, one should believe that His speech is an eternal attribute, nothing is like it; just as His essence is an eternal essence, nothing is like it; and as His essence is seen with a seeing that differs from the seeing of bodies and accidents and does not resemble them, so His speech is heard with a hearing that differs from letters and sounds and does not resemble them.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed refutation of the question 'how is Allah's speech heard?' Ghazali begins by stating that Allah's speech is not a letter or sound, so the question 'how' is fundamentally misapplied. He explains the nature of 'how' questions: they seek a likeness to something known. He uses the analogy of tasting sugar. There are two ways to answer such a question: either give the person direct experience (which is impossible for Allah's speech since only Prophet Moses heard it directly), or compare it to something similar (but nothing heard in this world resembles Allah's speech). He extends the analogy to a eunuch asking about intercourse or a deaf person asking about sound—they cannot truly understand because they lack the necessary experience. He then concludes that asking about the modality of hearing Allah's speech is as impossible as asking about the modality of seeing Allah in the afterlife, because God has no modality (kayfiyya). This does not negate the existence of the attribute; one must affirm that Allah's speech is an eternal attribute unlike any other, heard in a manner beyond human comprehension.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because we cannot explain how Allah's speech is heard, it must not exist.
Correction: Impossibility of explanation does not imply non-existence; it only indicates that the attribute transcends sensory experience.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the question 'how is Allah's speech heard?' impossible to answer?
A) Because Allah's speech is not a sound, so there is no basis for comparison.
B) Because the questioner lacks the necessary sense of hearing.
C) Because the answer would require direct experience of Allah's speech, which is impossible for most people, and nothing in creation resembles it.
D) Because Allah's speech is a created attribute and thus cannot be heard.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is an eternal attribute, heard in a manner beyond human comprehension; the impossibility of explaining 'how' does not negate its reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you doubted something about Allah because you couldn't grasp its 'how.' What would it mean for you to say, 'I believe, even though I cannot compare it to anything I know'?
?
If you were the eunuch in Ghazali's example, would you accept that some experiences are simply beyond your reach? In what areas of your faith do you need to submit to that same humility before Allah?

How Can Eternal Speech Be in a Physical Book?
Ghazali answers the objection that Allah's speech cannot be in physical books.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali resolves the apparent contradiction between the eternity of Allah's speech and its presence in physical copies of the Quran.

Core Idea
Allah's eternal speech is signified by the mushaf, not contained in it.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a common objection: if the Quran is the eternal speech of Allah, how can it be written in created books? He presents the objection clearly, then offers a precise distinction between the physical medium and the eternal attribute it indicates. This distinction is crucial for understanding how Muslims revere the mushaf while affirming the uncreated nature of Allah's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part where the opponent poses the dilemma. Notice the two horns: either the eternal speech is physically in the mushaf (impossible) or it is not (contradicting reverence).
2
Identify Ghazali's response
Read the response starting with 'We say'. Ghazali affirms that Allah's speech is written, preserved, and recited, but then clarifies that the physical components are created.
3
Understand the distinction
Focus on the key phrase: 'it does not follow that the indication and the indicated are in the mushaf'. This is the core of the argument.
4
Apply the analogy
Consider the example of 'fire is written in the book'. This analogy helps solidify the difference between a sign and what it signifies.
The analogy is from the passage itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستبعاد الثاني: أن يقال: كلام الله تعالى حال في المصاحف أم لا؟ فإن كان حالاً.. تحريجة: كيف حل القديم في المصاحف فكيف حلَّ القديم في المحدث؟ فإن قلتم: لا.. فهو بخلاف الإجماع؛ إذ احترام المصحف مجمع عليه؛ حتى حرم على المحدثِ مسُّه، وليس ذلك إلا لأن فيه كلام الله تعالى؟ فنقول: كلام الله تعالى مكتوب في المصاحف محفوظ في القلوب مقروء بالألسنة، وأما الكاغدُ والحبرُ والكتابة والحروفُ والأصوات كلُّها حادثة؛ لأنها أجسام وأعراض في أجسام، وكلُّ ذلك حادث، وإذا قلنا: هذا السؤال وارد ممَّن لم يفهم الفرق بين إنه مكتوب في المصحف؛ أعني: صفة القديم سبحانه.. لم يلزم أن يكون الدلالة والمدلول القديم في المصحف، كما أنَّا إذا قلنا: النارُ مكتوبة في الكتاب.. لم يلزم.
The second objection: That it may be said: Is the speech of Allah, may He be exalted, present in the mushafs or not? If it is present, then a problem: How can the eternal be present in the mushafs? And how can the eternal dwell in the temporal? If you say: No, then it contradicts the consensus, for the reverence of the mushaf is agreed upon; even touching it is forbidden for the one in a state of ritual impurity, and that is only because in it is the speech of Allah. We say: The speech of Allah is written in the mushafs, preserved in hearts, recited by tongues. As for the paper, ink, writing, letters, and sounds, they are all temporal, because they are bodies and accidents in bodies, and all of that is temporal. And when we say: This question is raised by one who does not understand the difference between [the fact that] it is written in the mushaf—I mean the attribute of the Eternal, glorified be He—it does not follow that the indication and the indicated (the eternal) are in the mushaf, just as when we say: 'Fire is written in the book,' it does not follow.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection regarding the presence of Allah's speech in physical copies of the Qur'an (mushafs). Ghazali presents the dilemma: If Allah's speech (which is eternal) is present in the mushaf (which is created), then how can the eternal dwell in the temporal? If it is not present, then why do Muslims revere the mushaf? Ghazali's response distinguishes between the physical medium and the meaning. He states that Allah's speech—the eternal attribute—is written in mushafs, preserved in hearts, and recited by tongues, but the paper, ink, writing, letters, and sounds are all created (temporal) things. The eternal speech is not physically indwelling in the mushaf; rather, the mushaf indicates (dalla) the eternal speech, just as when we say 'fire is written in a book,' we do not mean fire itself is inside the book. The reverence for the mushaf is due to it being the bearer (dāll) of the eternal attribute. Thus, there is no real contradiction. The objection arises from misunderstanding the relationship between the signifier and the signified.

المصحف
قديم
حادث
دلالة
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the physical mushaf literally contains Allah's eternal speech as a substance.
Correction: Ghazali explains that the physical components are created and merely indicate the eternal attribute; the mushaf is revered because it signifies Allah's speech, not because it contains it physically.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali resolve the objection that Allah's speech cannot be in physical mushafs?
A) He denies that Allah's speech is in the mushaf, claiming only the meaning is there.
B) He affirms that the eternal speech is physically present in the mushaf but in a non-spatial manner.
C) He distinguishes between the physical media (paper, ink) which are created, and the attribute of speech which is eternal and signified by them.
D) He argues that the mushaf is not actually holy, only the recitation is.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali resolves the objection by distinguishing between the created physical medium and the eternal attribute it signifies.

The Quran in the Mushaf: Eternal Speech vs. Created Signs
Ghazali answers the objection about the Quran in the mushaf by distinguishing eternal speech from its indicators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between the eternal speech of Allah and the created physical signs that indicate it, and why the mushaf is honored despite being created.

Core Idea
The Quran in the mushaf is a created indicator of the eternal speech, not the speech itself.

This lesson tackles a common objection: if the Quran is God's speech, how can it be in a physical book without making the Eternal temporal? Ghazali provides a clear and memorable answer, using the analogy of fire to illustrate the difference between an indicator and what it indicates. Let's trace his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the objection as presented by the hypothetical questioner. Notice the dilemma: either the eternal speech indwells in the mushaf (making it temporal) or it does not (contradicting the consensus on respect for the mushaf).
The objection is a classic theological puzzle about divine attributes and creation.
2
Identify Ghazali's core distinction
Ghazali responds by distinguishing between the eternal speech itself and its physical representations (paper, ink, letters, sounds). The speech is the meaning (madlul); the letters are indicators (dalala).
This distinction is key to understanding how the Quran can be both eternal in meaning and created in form.
3
Analyze the fire analogy
The analogy of fire clarifies the point: saying 'fire is written in the book' does not mean fire's essence is inside the book. Similarly, saying the Quran is in the mushaf does not mean God's eternal speech is literally inside it.
The analogy helps visualize the difference between the thing itself and its representation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستبعاد الثاني : أن يقال : كلام الله تعالى حال في المصاحف أم لا ؟ فإن كان حالاً .. تخرجة [؟]: كيف حل القديم في المصاحف فكيف حلَّ القديم في المحدث ؟ فإن قلتم : لا .. فهو بخلاف الإجماع ؛ إذ احترام المصحف مجمع عليه ؛ حتى حرم على المحدثِ مسُّه ، وليس ذلك إلا لأن فيه كلام الله تعالى ؟ فنقول : كلام الله تعالى مكتوب في المصاحف محفوظ في القلوب مقروء بالألسنة ، وأما الكاغدُ والحبرُ والكتابة والحروفُ والأصوات كلُّها حادثة ؛ لأنها أجسام وأعراض في أجسام ، وكلُّ ذلك حادث ، وإذا قلنا : هذا السؤال وارد ممَّن لم يفهم الفرق بين إنه مكتوب في المصحف ؛ أعني : صفة القديم سبحانه .. لم يلزم أن يكون الدلالة والمدلول القديم في المصحف ، كما أنَّا إذا قلنا : النارُ مكتوبة في الكتاب.. لم يلزم منه أن تكون ذاتُ النار حالةٌ فيه ؛ إذ لو حلَّتْ فيه .. لاحترق المصحف . ومن تكلم بالنار فلو كانت ذاتُ النار حالة بلسانه .. لاحترق لسانه ، فالنار جسم حار ، وعليه دلالة الأصواتُ المقطَّعةُ تقطيعاً يحصل منه : ( النون والألف والراء ) فالحارُ المحرقُ ذاتُ المدلول لا نفس الدلالة ، فكذلك الكلام القديم القائم بذات الله تعالى هو المدلول لا ذات الدليل ، والحروف أدلة ، وللأدلة حرمةٌ إذا جعل الشرعُ لها حرمةً ؛ فلذلك وجبَ احترام المصحف ؛ لأنَّ ما فيه دلالة على صفة الله تعالى
The second objection: It is said: Is the speech of Allah the Exalted 'indwelling' in the mushafs or not? If it is indwelling ... then a problem arises: How does the Eternal indwell in the mushafs, and how does the Eternal indwell in the temporal? If you say: No ... then it contradicts the consensus; for respecting the mushaf is agreed upon, even to the point that touching it is forbidden for one in a state of major impurity, and that is only because it contains the speech of Allah the Exalted? So we say: The speech of Allah the Exalted is written in the mushafs, preserved in hearts, recited by tongues. As for the paper, ink, writing, letters, and sounds, all of them are temporal; because they are bodies and accidents in bodies, and all of that is temporal. And if we say: This question comes from one who did not understand the difference between that it is written in the mushaf, meaning the attribute of the Eternal, exalted is He ... it does not entail that the indicator and the indicated are the eternal in the mushaf. Just as when we say: 'Fire is written in the book' ... it does not entail that the essence of fire is indwelling in it; for if it indwelt in it, the mushaf would burn. And if one speaks of fire, if the essence of fire were indwelling in his tongue, his tongue would burn. For fire is a hot body, and upon it there is indication through sounds that are cut with a cutting from which comes: (the nun, the alif, the ra). So the hot burning thing is the essence of the indicated, not the indication itself. Thus, the eternal speech subsisting in the essence of Allah the Exalted is the indicated, not the indication itself. Letters are indications, and indications have sanctity if the Law grants them sanctity; therefore, respecting the mushaf is obligatory, because what it contains is an indication of the attribute of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents and answers the second objection: Is God's speech literally in the mushaf (physical book)? The objection poses a dilemma: either God's speech indwells in the mushaf, making the Eternal temporal, or it does not, contradicting the consensus that the mushaf is respected because it contains God's speech. Ghazali's response distinguishes between the speech itself (the eternal attribute) and its physical representation. The paper, ink, and letters are all created (hadith), but they indicate (dalala) the eternal speech. The eternal speech is the meaning (madlul), not the physical letters. He uses the analogy of fire: when we say 'fire is written in the book,' we do not mean the fiery essence is inside the book; otherwise the book would burn. Similarly, the letters that spell 'fire' are mere indicators, not the fire itself. Thus, the eternal speech is the meaning indicated by the letters. The letters are created, but they have sanctity because the Law honors them as indicators of God's attribute. Therefore, respecting the mushaf is required.

Correction: Ghazali explains that the speech is eternal as a meaning in God's essence; the physical book is created. The mushaf is honored because it contains indicators of that eternal speech, not because the speech itself is in it.

D) Because fire does not exist in books

Key Takeaway
The mushaf is created and respected because it contains indicators of Allah's eternal speech, not because the speech itself is physically present.

Ghazali's Threefold Distinction: Qira'a, Maqru', and Quran
Ghazali distinguishes between qira'a, maqru', and Quran to resolve the createdness issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's terminological distinction between qira'a (recitation), maqru' (the recited), and Quran, and how this distinction resolves the question of whether the Quran is created.

Core Idea
Ghazali's threefold distinction saves the eternity of the Quran while acknowledging the createdness of recitation.

Ghazali faces an objection: if the Quran is God's speech, it cannot be created, but it is composed of letters and sounds which are created. To resolve this, he introduces three terms that separate the eternal attribute from its temporal expression. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice how Ghazali structures his response to the objection.
Pay attention to the three terms he introduces.
2
Identify the three terms
Find where Ghazali defines qira'a, maqru', and Quran. Understand each definition and its implications for createdness.
Maqru' is eternal; qira'a is temporal; Quran can refer to either.
3
Apply the distinction
Explain how this terminological distinction resolves the original objection. Why does the Salaf's statement that the Quran is uncreated remain valid?
The Quran as God's attribute is uncreated, while the human act of recitation is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستبعاد الثالث : قولهم : إنَّ القرآن كلام الله تعالى أم لا ؟ فإن قلتم : لا .. فقد خرجتم عن كون القرآن كلام الله عن الإجماع ، وإن قلتم : نعم .. فما هو سوى الحروف والأصوات ، وهو صوت وحرف؟ ومعلوم أن قراءة القارىء هي الحرف والصوت ؟ فنقول : ههنا ثلاثة ألفاظ : قراءة ، ومقروء ، وقرآن. أما المقروء : فهو كلام الله تعالى ؛ أعني : صفته القديمة القائمة بذاته. وأما القراءة : فهي في اللسان عبارةٌ عن فعل القارىء الذي ابتدأه بعد أن كان تاركاً له ، ولا معنى للحادث إلا أنه ابتدىء بعد أن لم يكن. فإن كان الخصم لا يفهم هذا من الحادث.. فلنترك لفظ الحادث والمخلوق ، ولكن نقول : القراءة فعل ابتدأه القارىء بعد أن لم يكن يفعله وهو محسوس. وأما القرآن : فقد يطلقُ ويراد به المقروء، فإن أريد به ذلك .. فهو قديم غير مخلوق ، وهو الذي أراده السلف بقولهم : القرآنُ : كلام الله تعالى غيرُ مخلوق (۱) ؛ أي : المقروء بالألسنة. وإن أريد به القراءة التي هي فعل القارىء ففعلُ القارىء لا يسبقُ وجودَ القارىء ، وما لا يسبق وجوده فهو حادث . . فهو حادث
The third objection: Their saying: Is the Quran the speech of Allah the Exalted or not? If you say: No ... then you have departed from the consensus that the Quran is the speech of Allah. And if you say: Yes ... then it is nothing but letters and sounds, and it is a sound and a letter? And it is known that the recitation of the reciter is the letter and the sound? So we say: Here are three terms: recitation (qira'a), recited (maqru'), and Quran (quran). As for the recited (maqru'), it is the speech of Allah the Exalted, meaning His eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. As for the recitation (qira'a), it is on the tongue an expression of the act of the reciter which he began after he had been refraining from it, and there is no meaning for the temporal (al-hadith) except that it began after it was not. If the opponent does not understand this from 'temporal'... then let us leave the term 'temporal' and 'created', but we say: The recitation is an act that the reciter began after he was not doing it, and it is sensible. As for the Quran (quran), it may be used and intended to mean the recited. If it is intended to mean that, then it is eternal, uncreated, and that is what the Salaf intended by their statement: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated' (1), meaning the recited by tongues. And if it is intended to mean the recitation which is the act of the reciter, then the act of the reciter does not precede the existence of the reciter, and what does not precede its existence is temporal... so it is temporal.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage answers the third objection: Is the Quran the speech of God or not? If it is, the opponent says it is only letters and sounds, which are created. Ghazali resolves this by distinguishing three terms: qira'a (recitation), maqru' (the recited), and quran (Quran). The maqru' is the eternal speech of God, His attribute. The qira'a is the act of the reader, which is created because it begins after not existing. The term 'Quran' can refer either to the recited (maqru')—and then it is eternal and uncreated, as the Salaf said—or to the recitation (qira'a), which is the act of the reader, and then it is created. This distinction preserves the doctrine that the Quran as God's speech is uncreated, while the physical recitation and written forms are created.

Correction: Ghazali clarifies that the essence of the Quran (as God's attribute) is uncreated, while the human act of recitation and the physical forms are created.
