The Fire Analogy: Distinguishing Divine Speech from Its Indications
Ghazali uses fire analogy to distinguish divine attribute from its indications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the analogy of fire to distinguish between Allah's eternal attribute of speech and the created letters and sounds of the Quran.

Core Idea
The letters of the Quran are signs, not the eternal speech itself.

This passage tackles a critical theological confusion: are the physical letters of the Quran eternal? Ghazali employs a clever analogy with fire to show that the letters are merely indications (dalālāt) of God's attribute of speech, not the attribute itself. Understanding this distinction is key to affirming God's timeless speech without attributing creation or temporality to His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical question at the beginning.
2
Identify the fire analogy
Ghazali presents a scenario: if the essence of fire were in the mushaf, the mushaf would burn. He also mentions that speaking the word 'fire' does not burn the tongue.
The key is that the word 'fire' is just a sign, not the fire itself.
3
Apply the analogy to divine speech
Just as the burning hot is the object of indication and not the indication itself, so God's eternal speech is the object indicated by the letters.
The letters (dalālāt) point to the eternal attribute (madlūl).
4
Understand the implication for the mushaf
Ghazali concludes that the mushaf deserves respect because it contains indications of God's attribute, not because the letters are themselves eternal.
This preserves the sanctity of the Quran while affirming its createdness in form.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can apply the analogy correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكيف التوفيق بين منه أن تكون ذاتُ النار حالةٌ فيه؛ إذ لو حلَّتْ فيه لاحترق المصحف. ومن تكلم بالنار فلو كانت ذاتُ النار حالة بلسانه لاحترق لسانه، فالنار جسم حار، وعليه دلالة الأصوات المقطَّعة تقطيعاً يحصل. منه: (النون والألف والراء) فالحارُ المحرقُ ذاتُ المدلول لا نفس الدلالة، فكذلك الكلام القديم القائم بذات الله تعالى هو المدلول لا ذات الدليل، والحروف أدلة، وللأدلة حرمةٌ إذا جعل الشرعُ لها حرمةً؛ فلذلك وجبَ احترام المصحف؛ لأنَّ ما فيه دلالة على صفة الله تعالى.
And how to reconcile between that? That the essence of fire is a state in it? Because if it had been in it, the mushaf would have burned. And whoever speaks with fire, if the essence of fire were on his tongue, his tongue would burn. Fire is a hot body, and upon it is the indication of the articulated letters articulatedly. From it: (the nūn, alif, rā). So the burning hot is the object of indication, not the indication itself. So likewise, the eternal speech subsisting in God's essence is the object of indication, not the indication itself. Letters are indications, and indications have sanctity if the Law gives them sanctity; therefore respecting the mushaf is obligatory because that which is in it indicates an attribute of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is an analogical argument defending the distinction between God's eternal attribute of speech and the created letters that indicate it. Ghazali begins with a hypothetical question: how can the essence of fire be said to 'reside' in something? If it did, the Qur'anic mushaf would burn when we recite the word 'fire,' and a speaker's tongue would burn when uttering it. The letters 'nūn, alif, rā' (the word 'nār') are mere indications (dalālāt) of the reality of fire, not the fire itself. Just as the burning fire is the object signified, not the sign, so God's eternal speech—an attribute subsisting in His essence—is the object signified, not the signs (the letters). The letters are created indications, and the Law grants them sanctity, so the mushaf is respected because it points to God's attribute. The core point: do not confuse the created medium (letters, sounds) with the uncreated attribute.

Key Terms
كلام قديم
مدلول
دلالة
حروف
مصحف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The physical letters and sounds of the Quran are themselves the eternal speech of God.
Correction: The letters are created indications (dalālāt) pointing to the uncreated attribute of speech, which subsists in God's essence.

Checkpoint

A) Because the word 'fire' has no meaning.
B) Because the letters are only indications of fire, not the essence of fire.
C) Because the mushaf is made of special fireproof material.
D) Because fire is not a real substance.

Key Takeaway
The eternal speech of Allah is an attribute of His essence, while the letters of the Quran are created indications pointing to that attribute.

The Three Terms: Recitation, Recited, and Quran
Ghazali defines three terms to resolve the creation controversy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between three terms—qirā'a, maqrū', and qur'ān—and why this distinction resolves the confusion over the createdness of the Quran.

Core Idea
The Quran is uncreated as God's attribute; our recitation is created.

In response to the objection that since the Quran consists of letters and sounds it must be created, Ghazali introduces a crucial terminological distinction. He separates the eternal divine attribute from the human act of recitation, and clarifies that the term 'Quran' can refer to either meaning. This lesson lays out the definitions of these three terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of Ghazali's three terms.
Notice the structured presentation: each term is defined sequentially.
2
Identify the three terms
Find the terms 'qirā'a', 'maqrū'', and 'qur'ān' in the passage. Note how each is defined.
3
Understand 'maqrū'' as eternal
Grasp that 'al-maqrū'' is the eternal attribute of God, not subject to creation.
This is the key to the Salaf's statement.
4
Distinguish recitation as an act
Recognize that 'al-qirā'a' is a human act that begins after not existing—hence created.
Ghazali uses 'ḥādith' (originated) to describe it.
5
Grasp the dual usage of 'Quran'
See that 'al-qur'ān' can refer to either the eternal recited or the created recitation depending on context.
The Salaf used it to mean the recited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستبعاد الثالث: قولهم: إن القرآن كلام الله تعالى أم لا؟ فإن قلتم: لا فقد خرجتم عن كون القرآن كلام الله عن الإجماع، وإن قلتم: نعم فما هو سوى الحروف والأصوات؟ ومعلوم أن قراءة القارئ هي الحرف والصوت؟ فنقول: ههنا ثلاثة ألفاظ: قراءة، ومقروء، وقرآن. أما المقروء: فهو كلام الله تعالى، أعني: صفته القديمة القائمة بذاته. وأما القراءة: فهي في اللسان عبارة عن فعل القارئ الذي ابتدأه بعد أن كان تاركاً له، ولا معنى للحادث إلا أنه ابتدىء بعد أن لم يكن. فإن كان الخصم لا يفهم هذا من الحادث فلنترك لفظ الحادث والمخلوق، ولكن نقول: القراءة فعل ابتدأه القارئ بعد أن لم يكن يفعله وهو محسوس. وأما القرآن: فقد يطلق ويراد به المقروء، فإن أريد به ذلك فهو قديم غير مخلوق، وهو الذي أراده السلف بقولهم: القرآن كلام الله تعالى غير مخلوق؛ أي: المقروء بالألسنة. وإن أريد به القراءة التي هي فعل القارئ ففعل القارئ لا يسبق وجود القارئ، وما لا يسبق وجود الحادث فهو حادث. وعلى الجملة: من يقول: ما أحدثته باختياري من الصوت وتقطيعه وكنت ساكتاً عنه قبله فهو قديم فلا ينبغي أن يخاطب ويكلم، بل ينبغي أن يعلم المسكين أنه ليس يدري ما يقوله، فلا هو يفهم معنى الحرف، ولا هو يعلم معنى الحادث، ولو علمهما لعلم أنه في نفسه إذا كان مخلوقاً كان ما يصدر عنه مخلوقاً، وعلم أن القديم لا ينتقل إلى ذات حادثة. فلنترك التطويل في الجليات؛ فإن قول القائل: (باسم الله) إن لم تكن السين فيه بعد الباء لم يكن قرآناً، بل كان خطأ، وإذا كان بعد غيره ومتأخراً عنه فكيف يكون قديماً؟ ونحن نريد بالقديم: ما لا يتأخر عن غيره أصلاً.
The third objection: Their saying: Is the Qur'an the speech of God or not? If you say no, then you have departed from the consensus regarding the Qur'an being the speech of God. And if you say yes, then it is nothing but letters and sounds? And it is known that the recitation of the reciter is the letter and sound? So we say: Here there are three terms: recitation (qirā'a), the recited (maqrū'), and the Qur'an (qur'ān). As for the recited: it is the speech of God, meaning His eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. As for recitation: it is on the tongue an expression of the act of the reciter which he began after having been refraining from it, and there is no meaning of the originated (al-ḥādith) except that it is begun after having not been. If the opponent does not understand this from the originated, then let us leave the term 'originated' and 'created', but we say: recitation is an act that the reciter began after not doing it, and it is perceptible. As for the Qur'an: it may be used to mean the recited, and if used for that, then it is eternal, uncreated, and that is what the Salaf intended by their saying: 'The Qur'an is the speech of God, uncreated', i.e., the recited by tongues. And if by it is meant the recitation which is the act of the reciter, then the act of the reciter does not precede the existence of the reciter, and whatever does not precede the existence of the originated, it is originated. In summary: Whoever says: 'What I originated by my choice of sound and its articulation, while I was silent about it before, is eternal'—such a person should not be addressed or spoken to. Rather, the poor fellow should be taught that he does not know what he says. For he neither understands the meaning of the letter nor the meaning of the originated. If he understood both, he would know that his own self, if it is created, then what issues from him is created, and he would know that the eternal does not transfer to a created essence. So let us leave extended discussion in clear matters. For the statement of the speaker: 'Bismillāh'—if the sīn in it is not after the bā', it would not be Qur'an, but rather a mistake. And if it is after another and delayed from it, then how can it be eternal? And we mean by 'eternal' (al-qadīm) that which does not come after anything else at all.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. Ghazali addresses the third objection from those who argue that since the Quran consists of letters and sounds—which are contingent—it must be created. He resolves the confusion by distinguishing three terms: (1) al-maqrū' (the recited) is the eternal attribute of God, (2) al-qirā'a (recitation) is the human act of uttering letters and sounds, which is originated, and (3) al-qur'ān can refer to either meaning. The Salaf used 'Quran' to mean the uncreated recited, not the created recitation. Ghazali then harshly refutes anyone who claims their own originated sound is eternal, calling them ignorant of the meaning of 'letter' and 'originated'. He gives an example: in 'Bismillāh', the word order matters; letters come after each other, so they cannot be eternal. The eternal is that which does not come after anything else. Thus, the Quran as God's attribute is uncreated, but human recitation is created.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran (as recited) is entirely created because it consists of letters and sounds.
Correction: The recited (maqrū') is an eternal attribute; only the human act of recitation is created.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, when the Salaf said 'The Qur'an is the speech of God, uncreated', what did they mean?
A) The physical book and ink are uncreated.
B) The letters and sounds uttered by the reciter are uncreated.
C) The recited (maqrū'), which is God's eternal attribute, is uncreated.
D) The recitation (qirā'a) is uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The Quran as God's eternal attribute is uncreated, while human recitation is a created act.

عندما تتلو القرآن، هل تفرق بين المعنى الذي تسمعه من لسانك (وهو فعل حادث) وبين المعنى الإلهي الذي تحمله الآيات (وهو كلام الله القديم)؟ كيف يؤثر هذا التفريق على خشوعك؟

Refuting the Confusion: Why Recitation Cannot Be Eternal
Ghazali refutes those who claim their recitation is eternal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of those who consider their recitation to be eternal, including the example of 'Bismillāh' and the argument that the eternal cannot be composed of successive parts.

Core Idea
If your recitation is originated by you, it cannot be eternal.

After establishing the terminological distinction, Ghazali turns to those who stubbornly claim that their recitation—an act they perform—is eternal. He dismisses such claims as ignorance of the meanings of 'letter' and 'originated', and provides a concrete example from the basmala to show that sequential letters cannot be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand Ghazali's harsh response.
Notice the tone: Ghazali says such a person should not be addressed.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the claim: 'What I originated by my choice of sound ... is eternal.' Why is this absurd?
3
Analyze the 'Bismillāh' example
See how the order of letters (bā' then sīn) shows sequence, which implies contingency.
The eternal cannot have parts that come after each other.
4
Understand the definition of 'eternal'
Grasp Ghazali's definition: the eternal is that which does not come after anything else at all.
This definition directly refutes the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستبعاد الثالث: قولهم: إن القرآن كلام الله تعالى أم لا؟ فإن قلتم: لا فقد خرجتم عن كون القرآن كلام الله عن الإجماع، وإن قلتم: نعم فما هو سوى الحروف والأصوات؟ ومعلوم أن قراءة القارئ هي الحرف والصوت؟ فنقول: ههنا ثلاثة ألفاظ: قراءة، ومقروء، وقرآن. أما المقروء: فهو كلام الله تعالى، أعني: صفته القديمة القائمة بذاته. وأما القراءة: فهي في اللسان عبارة عن فعل القارئ الذي ابتدأه بعد أن كان تاركاً له، ولا معنى للحادث إلا أنه ابتدىء بعد أن لم يكن. فإن كان الخصم لا يفهم هذا من الحادث فلنترك لفظ الحادث والمخلوق، ولكن نقول: القراءة فعل ابتدأه القارئ بعد أن لم يكن يفعله وهو محسوس. وأما القرآن: فقد يطلق ويراد به المقروء، فإن أريد به ذلك فهو قديم غير مخلوق، وهو الذي أراده السلف بقولهم: القرآن كلام الله تعالى غير مخلوق؛ أي: المقروء بالألسنة. وإن أريد به القراءة التي هي فعل القارئ ففعل القارئ لا يسبق وجود القارئ، وما لا يسبق وجود الحادث فهو حادث. وعلى الجملة: من يقول: ما أحدثته باختياري من الصوت وتقطيعه وكنت ساكتاً عنه قبله فهو قديم فلا ينبغي أن يخاطب ويكلم، بل ينبغي أن يعلم المسكين أنه ليس يدري ما يقوله، فلا هو يفهم معنى الحرف، ولا هو يعلم معنى الحادث، ولو علمهما لعلم أنه في نفسه إذا كان مخلوقاً كان ما يصدر عنه مخلوقاً، وعلم أن القديم لا ينتقل إلى ذات حادثة. فلنترك التطويل في الجليات؛ فإن قول القائل: (باسم الله) إن لم تكن السين فيه بعد الباء لم يكن قرآناً، بل كان خطأ، وإذا كان بعد غيره ومتأخراً عنه فكيف يكون قديماً؟ ونحن نريد بالقديم: ما لا يتأخر عن غيره أصلاً.

Resolving Contradictions: The Shared Meaning of 'Quran'
Ghazali argues for the ambiguity of 'Quran' to solve contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the concept of linguistic ambiguity (ishtirak) to reconcile the description of the Quran as both eternal and having created features.

Core Idea
The word 'Quran' is ambiguous, referring to both eternal speech and created recitation.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a key objection from the Mu'tazilites: how can the Quran be eternal if it has sections, verses, and is a miracle? He argues that the term 'Quran' is ambiguous, shared between the eternal recited speech and the act of recitation. Understanding this removes the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage from Ghazali's text carefully, then read the English translation. Note the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the objection and Ghazali's question
Find the Mu'tazilite objection about the Quran's features (suras, verses, miracle) and Ghazali's initial question about ambiguity.
The question is: 'Do you deny that the word 'Quran' is shared between recitation and the recited?'
3
Follow the two-case analysis
Ghazali considers two possibilities: if the opponent admits ambiguity, contradiction dissolves; if they deny it, he provides evidence that 'Quran' is used for both meanings.
Look for the poetic and hadith evidence for the second meaning (recitation).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the ambiguity (ishtirak) is the key to resolving the apparent contradiction between the Quran's eternal and created attributes.
Failure to grasp this leads to false perceptions of contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون للقديم مقاطع ومفاتح ؟ وكيف ينقسم بالسور والآيات ؟ وكيف يكونُ القديم معجزة الرسول والمعجزة هي فعل خارق للعادة ، وكل فعل فهو مخلوق ، فكيف يكون كلام الله تعالى قديما ؟
قلنا : أتنكرونَ أنَّ لفظ القرآن مشترك بين القراءة والمقروء أم لا ؟ فإن اعترفتم به .. فكلُّ ما أورده المسلمون من وصف القرآن بما هو قديم كقولهم : القرآن كلام الله تعالى غيرُ مخلوق .. أرادوا به المقروء ، وكلُّ ما وصفوه به مما لا يحتمله القديم ؛ ككونه سوراً وآيات ولها مقاطع ومفاتح .. أرادوا به العباراتِ الدالة على الصفة القديمة التي هي قرآن وإذا صار الاسم مشتركاً .. امتنع التناقض ، فالإجماع منعقد على أن لا قديم إلا الله تعالى ، والله تعالى يقول : {حَتَّى عَادَ كَالْعُرْجُونِ الْقَدِيمِ} ولكن نقول : اسم القديم مشترك بين معنيين ، فإذا ثبت من وجه .. لم يستحل نفيه من وجه آخر ، فكذا اسم القرآن ، وهو جواب عن كل ما يرددونه من الإطلاقات المتناقضة .
فإن أنكروا كونه مشتركاً .. فنعلم قطعاً إطلاقه لإرادة المقروء ، ودلّ عليه كلامُ السلفِ أَنَّ القرآنَ كلامُ الله سبحانه غيرُ مخلوق مع العلم بأنهم وأصواتهم وقراءتهم وأفعالهم كل ذلك مخلوق .
وأما إطلاقه لإرادة القراءة .. فقد قال الشاعر :
ضَحُوا بِأَشْمَطَ عُنْوانُ السُّجُودِ بِهِ يُقَطِّعُ اللَّيْلَ تَسْبيحا وَقُرْآنا
يعني : القراءة ، وقال رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : « ما أَذِنَ اللهُ لشيء إذنه لنبي حسن الصوت بالقرآن » والترنم يكون بالقراءة ، وقال كافة السلف : القرآنُ كلامُ الله غيرُ مخلوق ، وقالوا : القرآن معجزة وهي فعل الله تعالى ؛ إذ علموا أنَّ القديم لا يكون معجزاً ؛ فبانَ أَنَّهُ اسم مشترك ، ومن لم يفهم اشتراك اللفظ .. ظنَّ تناقضاً في هذه الإطلاقات
Can the eternal have sections and openings? How is it divided into suras and verses? How can the eternal be the miracle of the Messenger, while the miracle is an act breaking the custom, and every act is created? How then can the speech of Allah Most High be eternal? We said: Do you deny that the word 'Quran' is shared between the recitation (al-qira'a) and the recited (al-maqru') or not? If you admit it, then everything that the Muslims have mentioned describing the Quran as being eternal, like their saying 'The Quran is the speech of Allah Most High, uncreated,' they meant by it the recited (al-maqru'), and everything they have described it by which the eternal cannot bear, like being suras and verses having sections and openings, they meant by it the expressions (al-'ibarat) indicating the eternal attribute which is Quran. And when the name becomes shared, contradiction is prevented. For the consensus is held that nothing is eternal except Allah Most High, and Allah Most High says: 'until it became like an old date-stalk.' But we say: The name 'eternal' (al-qadim) is shared between two meanings, so if it is established from one aspect, its negation from another aspect is not impossible. So too the name 'Quran'. And this is an answer to all the contradictory usages they repeat. Then if they deny it being shared, we know with certainty its use to mean the recited (al-maqru'), and the speech of the Salaf indicates that: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated,' while knowing that they, their voices, their recitations, and their acts are all created. As for its use to mean recitation (al-qira'a), the poet said: 'They sacrificed a gray-haired man, on him the mark of prostration, he spends the night in glorification and Quran,' meaning recitation. And the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'Allah has not permitted anything as He permits a prophet with a beautiful voice in the Quran,' and humming is with recitation. And all the Salaf said: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated,' and they said: 'The Quran is a miracle (mu'jiza) and it is an act of Allah,' since they knew that the eternal cannot be a miracle. So it became clear that it is a shared name, and whoever does not understand the ambiguity of the word supposes contradiction in these usages.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's response to the Mu'tazilite objection that the Quran cannot be eternal because it has features like sections, verses, and being a miracle, which belong to created things. Ghazali begins by asking whether the term 'Quran' is ambiguous (mushtarak) between the recitation (al-qira'a, the act of reading) and the recited (al-maqru', the eternal speech). If the opponent admits ambiguity, then all descriptions of the Quran as eternal apply to the recited, while descriptions like 'suras and verses' apply to the expressions that indicate the eternal attribute. Once the name is shared, contradiction is impossible. Ghazali supports this by noting that 'eternal' (qadim) itself is ambiguous between Allah and other things (like the old date-stalk in the verse). Then he considers the case of denial that the term is shared: we know with certainty that 'Quran' is used for the recited, as the Salaf said it is uncreated even while their voices and acts are created. To prove the second meaning (recitation), he provides poetic evidence (the verse by Hassan ibn Thabit) and prophetic hadith (about permission for a beautiful voice in the Quran). The Salaf also said the Quran is a miracle, which is a created act, showing they used the term for the expression. Thus the term is shared, and failure to grasp this leads to the false perception of contradiction.

Key Terms
اشتراك
قرآن
مقروء
قراءة
قديم
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'Quran' always refers to the eternal speech alone.
Correction: Ghazali shows it is used for both the eternal recited speech and the created recitation.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the key to resolving the apparent contradictions about the Quran's nature?
A) Accepting that the Quran is created
B) Recognizing that the term 'Quran' is ambiguous (mushtarak)
C) Denying that the Quran can be divided into suras and verses
D) Claiming that the Quran is both eternal and created

Key Takeaway
The ambiguity of the term 'Quran' removes the apparent contradiction between its eternal and created attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read or recite the Quran, do you consciously distinguish between the eternal divine speech and your own created act of recitation? How might this awareness transform your reverence and humility in that moment?
?
Ghazali shows that misunderstanding a term leads to false contradictions. Are there any beliefs or doubts you hold about Islam that might stem from assuming a word has only one meaning? What would it mean for you to investigate that?
?
The Salaf affirmed the Quran as both uncreated and a miracle without discomfort. Is there a tension in your faith that you have been avoiding rather than seeking deeper understanding? What step can you take this week to resolve it through study and trust in Allah's wisdom?

The Fifth Objection: How Can Eternal Speech Be Heard?
Ghazali answers the objection that only sounds are heard, yet the Quran is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the eternity of the Quran against the objection that it is heard as sounds.

Core Idea
What Moses heard was the eternal attribute; what we hear are sounds that indicate it.

Ghazali addresses the fifth objection: if the Quran is heard in the world as sounds, how can it be eternal? He uses a comparison with Moses to show that the polytheist hearing the Quran does not hear the eternal speech itself, but only sounds that point to it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's initial question
Start by reading the objection stating that only sounds are heard, yet the Quran is heard. Then note Ghazali's question: if the polytheist hears the eternal speech itself, how is Moses distinguished?
The objection is based on the verse about granting protection so the polytheist can hear Allah's speech.
2
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Ghazali argues that if the polytheist hears the eternal speech itself, then Moses has no special virtue as 'kalim Allah'. This shows the absurdity of the objection.
3
Grasp the distinction between attribute and indicator
The only answer is to say that Moses heard the eternal attribute subsisting in Allah, while the polytheist hears created sounds that indicate that attribute. This is the key point.
Thus the term 'kalam' is ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستبعاد الخامس :
أن يقال : معلومٌ أَنَّهُ لا مسموع الآن إلا الأصوات ، وكلام الله تعالى مسموع الآن بالإجماع، وبدليل قوله تعالى : {وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ المُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجْرُهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَامَ اللَّهِ} فكيف يكون قديماً؟
فنقول : إن كان الصوتُ المسموع للمشرك عند الإجارة هو كلام الله تعالى القديم القائم بذاته .. فأي فضل لموسى عليه السلام في اختصاصه بكونه كليماً على المشركين وهم يسمعون كما سمع ؟! وهل يتصوَّرُ عن هذا جواب إلا أن يقال : مسموع موسى عليه السلام صفة قديمة قائمة بالله تعالى ، ومسموع المشركِ أصوات دالة على تيك الصفة ، ونتبيَّن به على القطع الاشتراك :
إما في اسم الكلام ، وهو تسمية الدلالات باسم المدلولات؛ فإن الكلام
The fifth improbability: It is said: It is known that nothing is heard now except sounds, and the speech of Allah Most High is heard now by consensus, and by the evidence of His saying {And if anyone of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the speech of Allah}. So how can it be eternal? We say: If the sound heard by the polytheist at the time of protection is the eternal speech of Allah existing in His essence, then what virtue does Moses (peace be upon him) have in his special distinction of being the one spoken to (kalim) over the polytheists, while they hear as he heard? And can there be conceived any answer to this except to say: What Moses heard is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah Most High, and what the polytheist hears are sounds indicating that attribute. And thereby we ascertain with certainty the ambiguity: either in the name of speech (kalam), which is calling the indications by the name of the indicated things; for speech...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage deals with the fifth objection: If only sounds are heard in this world, yet the Quran is eternal and is heard (as per the verse 'until he hears the speech of Allah'), how can the eternal be heard? Ghazali first states the objection: the Mu'tazila argue that the Quran is created because it is heard as sounds. He then offers a reductio ad absurdum: If the polytheist hears the very eternal speech itself, then Moses (peace be upon him) has no special virtue as 'kalim Allah' (the one spoken to by Allah), because the polytheist would hear the same eternal speech. The only way out is to distinguish: what Moses heard is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah, whereas what the polytheist hears are created sounds that indicate that attribute. Thus we establish that the term 'speech' (kalam) is ambiguous: it can refer to the eternal attribute or to the created sounds that indicate it. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely concluding that the expression is called 'kalam' metaphorically by naming the indicator by the name of the indicated.

Key Terms
استبعاد
مسموع
كلام
كليم
صوت
دلالة
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When we hear the Quran recited, we are directly hearing Allah’s eternal speech.
Correction: We are hearing created sounds that indicate the eternal speech, not the speech itself.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, the distinction between Moses hearing Allah's speech and a polytheist hearing the Quran is:
A) Moses heard sounds while the polytheist heard the eternal attribute
B) Moses heard an eternal attribute, while the polytheist hears sounds indicating that attribute
C) Both hear the same eternal speech directly
D) The polytheist hears a created sound, but Moses heard nothing

Key Takeaway
The term 'speech' is ambiguous: it can mean the eternal attribute or the created sounds that indicate it.

Answering the Fifth Objection: How Can Eternal Speech Be Heard Now?
Ghazali refutes the objection that eternal speech cannot be heard now by distinguishing two homonymies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's answer to the objection that eternal speech cannot be heard now, by distinguishing between hearing the eternal attribute and hearing created sounds that indicate it.

Core Idea
Eternal speech is not heard directly; only its created indications are heard.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a subtle objection: if God's speech is eternal, how can it be heard by temporal beings like Moses or a polytheist? He argues that the objector confuses two meanings of 'speech' and 'heard'. By carefully distinguishing between the uncreated attribute and the created sounds that indicate it, Ghazali preserves both the eternity of God's speech and the reality of its being heard.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Pay attention to how the objector uses the verse about the polytheist to challenge the eternity of God's speech.
2
Identify the dilemma
Notice that if the polytheist hears the same eternal speech as Moses, then Moses loses his unique status. Why is this a problem?
3
Understand Ghazali's answer
Focus on the distinction between what Moses heard (the eternal attribute itself) and what the polytheist hears (created sounds indicating it).
4
Explore the two types of homonymy
Ghazali gives two ways the terms 'speech' and 'heard' can be ambiguous. Trace each analogy: 'the knowledge of so-and-so' and 'the prince's speech via messenger'.
5
Connect to the doctrine of inner speech
Reflect on how this defense upholds the Ash'ari view that God's speech is an eternal attribute (kalām al-nafs), not literal sounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستبعاد الخامس : أن يقال : معلومٌ أَنَّهُ لا مسموع الآن إلا الأصوات ، وكلام الله تعالى تحريجة كلامه سبحانه مموعو مسموع الآن بالإجماع، وبدليل قوله تعالى : (وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ المُشركين تكيف يكون قديما بيا اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجْرُهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَّمَ اللَّهِ ؟ قديماً؟ فنقول : إن كان الصوتُ المسموع للمشرك عند الإجارة هو كلام الله إن كان المشرك يسمع كلام الله تعالى القديم القائم بذاته .. فأي فضل لموسى عليه السلام في اختصاصه فأي فضل لكليم الله بكونه كليماً على المشركين وهم يسمعون كما سمع ؟! وهل يتصوَّرُ عن هذا موسی علیه السلام؟ جواب إلا أن يقال : مسموع موسى عليه السلام صفة قديمة قائمة بالله تعالى ، ومسموع المشركِ أصوات دالة على تيك الصفة ، ونتبيَّن به على القطع الاشتراك : إما في اسم الكلام ، وهو تسمية الدلالات باسم المدلولات ؛ فإن الكلام هو كلام النفس تحقيقاً ، ولكن الألفاظ لدلالتها عليه أيضاً تسمى كلاماً كما تسمى علماً ؛ إذ يقال : سمعتُ علم فلان ، وإنما تسمع كلامه الدال على علمه. وإما في اسم المسموع ؛ فإن المفهوم المعلوم بسماع غيره قد يسمى مسموعاً ؛ كما يقال : سمعتُ كلامَ الأمير على لسان رسوله ، ومعلوم أن كلام الأمير لا يقومُ بلسان رسوله ، بل المسموع كلام الرسول الدال على كلام الأمير ، فهذا ما أردنا أن نذكرَهُ في إيضاح مذهب أهل السنة في كلام النفس المعدود من الغوامض ، وبقية أحكام الكلام نذكرها عند التعرُّضِ لأحكام الصفاتِ من القسم الثاني إن شاء الله تعالى
The fifth objection: It is said: It is known that nothing is heard now except sounds, and the speech of God Almighty... how can the eternal be heard now? It is heard by consensus, and by the evidence of His statement: 'And if any of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the word of God.' How can it be eternal? So we say: If the sound heard by the polytheist when granted protection is the speech of God — if the polytheist hears the eternal speech of God subsisting in His essence — then what virtue does Moses (peace be upon him) have in his distinction as the speaker with God? For the polytheists would hear as he heard! And is this conceivable regarding Moses? The answer is that it must be said: What Moses heard is an eternal attribute subsisting in God, and what the polytheist hears are sounds indicating that attribute. And we thereby ascertain with certainty the homonymy: either in the noun 'speech' (al-kalām), which is naming the indications by the name of the indicated things — for speech is truly inner speech (kalām al-nafs), but the words, because they indicate it, are also called speech, just as they are called 'knowledge' (ʿilm), for it is said: 'I heard the knowledge of so-and-so,' but you only hear his speech indicating his knowledge — or in the noun 'heard' (al-masmūʿ), for the meaning known by hearing something else may be called 'heard,' just as it is said: 'I heard the speech of the prince from the tongue of his messenger,' and it is known that the prince's speech does not subsist in the tongue of his messenger, but what is heard is the messenger's speech indicating the prince's speech. This is what we wanted to mention in clarifying the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna concerning inner speech (kalām al-nafs), which is counted among the subtle matters. We will mention the remaining rulings on speech when we discuss the attributes in the second section, God willing.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (istibʿād) and a final clarification. Ghazali presents the fifth objection: The objector argues that only sounds are heard, yet the Qur'an is heard now and is called the speech of God; but if it is eternal, how can it be heard? He supports this with the verse that a polytheist may hear the word of God. The objection then sharpens: If the polytheist actually hears the eternal speech itself, then Moses has no special distinction as 'the one spoken to' (Kalīm Allāh), because the polytheist would hear the same eternal speech. Ghazali's answer distinguishes between what Moses heard and what the polytheist hears. Moses heard the eternal attribute (al-ṣifa al-qadīma) subsisting in God's essence, whereas the polytheist hears only sounds that indicate that attribute. This shows that there is homonymy (ishtirāk) in either of two terms: (1) in the term 'speech' (kalām) — the sounds are called 'speech' because they indicate the true inner speech (kalām al-nafs), just as one says 'I heard so-and-so's knowledge' but actually hears his speech indicating knowledge; (2) in the term 'heard' (masmūʿ) — the meaning understood from hearing something can itself be called 'heard', as one says 'I heard the prince's speech from the tongue of his messenger,' though the prince's speech is not in the messenger's tongue. Ghazali then concludes that this is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna regarding inner speech (kalām al-nafs), which is a subtle matter, and he promises to complete the discussion of the attributes later. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that the eternal speech itself becomes auditory sound; rather, it is the created sounds that indicate it.

Key Terms
الاستبعاد الخامس (al-istibʿād al-khāmis)
كلام النفس (kalām al-nafs)
المسموع (al-masmūʿ)
الصفة القديمة (al-ṣifa al-qadīma)
الاشتراك (al-ishtirāk)
الدلالة (al-dalāla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternal speech must be directly audible as sounds, otherwise it cannot be heard at all.
Correction: Ghazali explains that what is actually heard are created sounds that indicate the uncreated attribute; the attribute itself is not heard with the ears.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the difference between what Moses heard and what a polytheist hears when granted protection?
A) Both hear the same eternal speech directly.
B) Moses heard the eternal attribute, while the polytheist hears created sounds indicating it.
C) The polytheist hears a different, created speech of God.
D) Moses heard sounds, while the polytheist hears the eternal attribute.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends the eternity of God's speech by showing that it is heard indirectly through created sounds that indicate the uncreated attribute, resolving the apparent paradox.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you believe you are hearing the eternal speech of God, or merely its created indication? How does this affect your reverence and attentiveness? What can you do today to hear it as if it were the direct address of God to you?
?
If the polytheist hears only the indication, but Moses hears the eternal attribute, what does this say about the privilege of prophethood? How should you respond to the privilege of having the Qur'an in your hands? Are you treating it as a mere book of sounds or as the very speech of the Lord?
?
Ghazali distinguishes between the created sound and the uncreated meaning. In your daily recitation, do you rush through the sounds without pondering the meaning? What would it take for you to hear 'God's speech' in both the words and what they indicate?

Divine Attributes

Attributes Are Additional to the Essence
Ghazali states the first ruling on divine attributes and refutes opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first ruling that the seven divine attributes are real and additional to the essence yet not separate entities, and how he refutes the objections of the Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Core Idea
Attributes are not the essence, but they are not separate gods.

After establishing that God has attributes, Ghazali now clarifies their relationship to the divine essence. The first ruling is crucial: attributes are real and distinct from the essence, yet they are not separate gods or eternal entities independent of God. This lesson presents Ghazali's statement, the objections raised by rationalist theologians and philosophers, and his sharp rebuttal pointing out their inconsistency. Understanding this ruling helps avoid both extremes of denying attributes and of multiplying eternal beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then its literal translation. Notice how Ghazali announces four rulings but only gives the first here.
The passage is the opening of a longer discussion.
2
Identify the first ruling
Ghazali states that the seven attributes are 'za'ida 'ala al-dhat' (additional to the essence) but 'duna mughayara' (without being separate). Note the dual affirmation.
The term 'duna mughayara' is key to avoiding the charge of polytheism.
3
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazila and philosophers deny real attributes. They argue that only one eternal being exists and that attributes are just states (ahwal).
Their proof is that the evidence only shows God is knowing, not that He has knowledge.
4
Spot the contradiction
Ghazali points out that the Mu'tazila themselves affirm will and speech as additional attributes, contradicting their own denial. Trace this in the text.
This inconsistency dismantles their position.
5
Reflect on the balance
Consider how Ghazali's position maintains God's oneness while affirming real attributes. Why is this balance important?
It avoids both ta'til (stripping God of attributes) and tashbih (comparing God to creation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني من هذا القطب في أحكام الصفات عامة ، ما يشترك فيها أو يفتري وهي أربعة أحكام : الحكم الأول : أنَّ الصفاتِ السبعة التي دللنا عليها ليست هي الذات ، بل هي زائدة على الذات ، فصانع العالم عالم بعلم ، حي بحياةٍ ، قادر بقدرة ، هكذا في جميع الصفات زيادة الصفات على الذات دون مغايرة وذهبت المعتزلة والفلاسفة إلى إنكار ذلك ، وقالوا : القديم ذات واحدة ، ولا يجوز إثبات ذوات قديمة متعددة ، وإنما الدليل يدلُّ على كونه عالماً قادراً حياً ، لا على العلم والقدرة والحياة • ولنعينِ العلم من الصفات حتى لا نحتاج إلى تكرير جميع الصفات ؛ وزعموا أن العالمية حال للذات وليست بصفة (۱) ، لكن المعتزلة ناقضوا في صفتين إذ قالوا : إنه مريد بإرادة زائدة على الذات، ومتكلم بكلام زائد على الإرادة والكلام
The second section of this pole: concerning the rulings of attributes in general, what they share or differ in. They are four rulings. The first ruling: the seven attributes that we have demonstrated are not the essence; rather they are additional to the essence. So the Maker of the world is knowing by knowledge, living by life, powerful by power. Thus in all attributes: the attributes are additional to the essence without being separate (duna mughayara). The Mu'tazila and philosophers denied that, and said: the Eternal is one essence, and it is impermissible to posit multiple eternal entities (dhawat). The proof only indicates that He is knowing, powerful, living, not [the existence of] knowledge, power, and life. Let us single out knowledge among the attributes so we do not need to repeat all attributes. They claimed that 'being knowing' (al-ʿālimiyya) is a state (ḥāl) for the essence, not an attribute. However, the Mu'tazila contradicted themselves concerning two attributes, for they said: He is willing by a will additional to the essence, and speaking by speech additional to will and speech.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage begins the second section of the 'pole' (al-quṭb) on the divine attributes. Ghazali announces that there are four general rulings; he then presents the first ruling: the seven attributes (life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, speech) are not identical with the essence (dhat) but are additional to it—real and distinct, yet not separate (duna mughayara, meaning they are not other entities independent of the essence). He gives examples: God is knowing by knowledge, living by life, powerful by power. The Mu'tazila and philosophers denied this, arguing that the eternal is a single essence and that positing multiple eternal 'things' (dhawat) is forbidden. They claimed that the proofs only establish that God is knowing, etc., not that there are distinct attributes. They reinterpret 'knowing' as a state (ḥāl) rather than a real attribute. However, Ghazali points out a contradiction: the Mu'tazila affirm that God is willing (murīd) by a will additional to the essence, and speaking (mutakallim) by speech additional to will and speech. Thus they inconsistently accept the reality of will and speech while denying other attributes.

Key Terms
الصفات
الذات
الزيادة
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة
الحال
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That saying attributes are 'additional' implies there are multiple eternal gods.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly adds 'duna mughayara' (without being separate) to show that attributes are not independent entities but real distinctions within the one essence.

Checkpoint
What is the first ruling regarding divine attributes according to Ghazali?
A) Attributes are identical to the essence.
B) Attributes are additional to the essence without being separate.
C) Attributes are merely states (ahwal).
D) Attributes are eternal entities independent of the essence.

Key Takeaway
The first ruling on divine attributes: they are real and added to the essence, yet not separate entities — a balanced position between denial and polytheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing,' do you truly believe that His knowledge is a real, eternal attribute distinct from His essence, or do you reduce it to a mere description? How does your understanding affect the way you seek His knowledge in your life?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected additional attributes to protect tawhid, yet they contradicted themselves. Have you ever defended a position about God that, upon reflection, contains an inconsistency you ignored for comfort? Will you examine your own beliefs today?
?
Ghazali affirms that attributes are additional 'without separation' (duna mughayara). How do you hold together the reality of God's attributes and the unity of His essence in your heart when you pray? Are you willing to sit with the mystery rather than force a simplistic solution?

The First Ruling: Attributes Are Additional to the Essence
Al-Ghazali states the first ruling on attributes and the opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that God's attributes are additional to His essence without implying separateness, and the opposing view of the Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Core Idea
Attributes are real but not separate—they add to the essence without making many essences.

Al-Ghazali begins the section on rulings on divine attributes with a foundational claim: the seven attributes are not identical to the essence but are additional. This is a key point of Sunni theology that distinguishes it from other schools. Let's read his statement and the objection he reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'دون مغايرة' (without differentiation) – it is crucial.
2
Identify the Sunni position
Identify the claim: attributes are 'zā'ida ʿalā al-dhāt' (additional to the essence). Notice the examples: knowledge, life, power.
Contrast this with the Mu'tazila view mentioned next.
3
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the Mu'tazila and philosophers argue that affirming multiple eternal attributes implies multiple eternal essences, which is impossible.
This objection sets up the need for further explanation in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني من هذا القطب في أحكام الصفات عامة ، ما يشترك فيها أو يفتري وهي أربعة أحكام : الحكم الأول : أنَّ الصفاتِ السبعة التي دللنا عليها ليست هي الذات ، بل هي زائدة على الذات ، فصانع العالم عالم بعلم ، حي بحياةٍ ، قادر بقدرة ، هكذا في جميع الصفات زيادة الصفات على الذات دون مغايرة وذهبت المعتزلة والفلاسفة إلى إنكار ذلك ، وقالوا : القديم ذات واحدة ، ولا يجوز إثبات ذوات قديمة متعددة ، وإنما الدليل يدلُّ على كونه عالماً قادراً حياً ، لا على العلم والقدرة والحياة
The second part of this pole concerning the rulings on attributes in general, what is common or distinct among them. And they are four rulings: The first ruling: that the seven attributes which we have demonstrated are not the essence, rather they are additional to the essence. So the Maker of the world is knowing by knowledge, living by life, powerful by power, likewise in all attributes: the increase of attributes over the essence without differentiation. And the Mu'tazila and philosophers rejected that, and said: The Eternal is one essence, and it is not permissible to affirm multiple eternal essences. Rather, the evidence indicates that He is knowing, powerful, living, not upon knowledge, power, and life.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the first ruling on the divine attributes and the opposing view. Al-Ghazali begins by introducing the second part of the second pole, which deals with the attributes generally, noting that there are four rulings. The first ruling is that the seven attributes (previously demonstrated) are not identical to the essence but are additional to it, without implying separateness or otherness. He gives examples: God is knowing by knowledge, living by life, powerful by power. Then he presents the objection of the Mu'tazila and philosophers: they argue that the Eternal is a single essence and that affirming multiple eternal entities (attributes) is invalid. They claim that the evidence only proves that God is knowing, powerful, and living, not that He has separate attributes of knowledge, power, and life.

معتزلة

Correction: Al-Ghazali explicitly says 'without differentiation' (دون مغايرة), meaning the attributes are not separate entities but are real and additional in a way that does not imply multiplicity of essences.

?

The Mu'tazila Contradiction on Will and Speech
Al-Ghazali points out the Mu'tazila inconsistency regarding will and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ghazali exposes the Mu'tazila's self-contradiction in affirming will and speech as additional attributes while denying other added attributes.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila are inconsistent: they deny added attributes for knowledge but affirm them for will and speech.

Al-Ghazali begins by noting that the Mu'tazila consider knowledge to be a state (hal) rather than an attribute. Yet, he points out a striking inconsistency: they affirm two attributes—will and speech—as additional to the essence, albeit with special conditions. This passage reveals a key weakness in the Mu'tazila position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: first a remark about knowledge, then the contradiction in will and speech.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two affirmed attributes
Find where Al-Ghazali states that the Mu'tazila affirm will and speech as additional to the essence. Note the special conditions: will is created in no locus, speech is created in a body.
3
Understand the contradiction
Ask yourself: why is this a contradiction? The Mu'tazila generally deny additional attributes (e.g., knowledge is a state), yet they make exceptions for will and speech. This undermines their rational consistency.
Consider how this relates to their overall theological system.
4
Reflect on the significance
This critique shows that the Mu'tazila position is not perfectly coherent. Al-Ghazali uses this as a point of attack to argue for the Ash'ari view of eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنعينِ العلم من الصفات حتى لا نحتاج إلى تكرير جميع الصفات ؛ وزعموا أن العالمية حال للذات وليست بصفة (١) ، لكن المعتزلة ناقضوا في صفتين إذ قالوا : إنه مريد بإرادة زائدة على الذات، ومتكلم بكلام زائد على الإرادة والكــــــلام الذات ، إلا أن الإرادة يخلقها في غير محل ، والكلام يخلقه في جسم ويكون هو المتكلم به (١) . صفتان ثابتان عند المعتزلة بخلاف الفلاسفة
And let us specify knowledge among the attributes so that we do not need to repeat all attributes. And they claimed that being knowing is a state (hal) of the essence and not an attribute (1). But the Mu'tazila contradicted in two attributes, for they said: He is willing by a will additional to the essence, and speaking by speech additional to the will and speech [additional to] the essence, except that He creates the will in a non-locus, and He creates speech in a body and He is the speaker by it (1). Two attributes affirmed according to the Mu'tazila contrary to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazila position. Al-Ghazali first specifies knowledge as an example to avoid repetition, noting that the Mu'tazila consider 'knowing' to be a state (hal) of the essence rather than a real attribute. However, he points out their inconsistency: they affirm two attributes—will and speech—as additional to the essence. They say God is willing by a will additional to the essence, and speaking by speech additional to the essence and to the will, but with the qualification that the will is created in no locus (i.e., not subsisting in a body) and speech is created in a body (e.g., the burning bush) and God is the speaker by that created speech. This shows that the Mu'tazila do affirm some added attributes, contradicting their denial of added attributes for knowledge, power, etc., and thus their position is self-contradictory.

Key Terms
علم
حال
صفة
إرادة
كلام
معتزلة
فلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila consistently deny all added attributes; they actually affirm will and speech as additional.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that the Mu'tazila affirm will and speech as additional attributes, contradicting their general position that attributes are mere states.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge and power.
B) Will and speech.
C) Life and hearing.
D) Power and will.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila contradict themselves by affirming will and speech as additional attributes while denying the same for knowledge, power, etc.

The Decisive Proof: 'Knowing' Entails Knowledge
Ghazali's decisive proof that 'alim entails 'ilm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that affirming God is 'alim necessarily affirms the attribute of 'ilm, and why this does not imply a separate intermediate state.

Core Idea
To say God knows is to say God has knowledge.

Ghazali now presents the decisive proof that knowledge is a real attribute of God, distinct from His essence. He argues that anyone who says 'God knows' has ipso facto affirmed that God has knowledge. Yet some theologians, misled by language, imagine that 'knowing' implies a third thing called a 'state'. Ghazali cuts through this confusion with a simple linguistic analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's decisive proof
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali states the proof concisely: whoever affirms 'alim has affirmed 'ilm.
The proof is called 'decisive' because it relies on immediate conceptual understanding.
2
Understand the two expressions
Identify the long expression (the essence has knowledge) and the short expression (the essence is knowing). Ghazali uses the sandal analogy to show they mean the same thing.
The analogy: 'his foot is in the sandal' and 'he is sandaled' are equivalent.
3
Refute the 'state' misconception
Ghazali explicitly denies that the subsistence of knowledge creates a separate 'state' called 'alimiyya. Instead, knowledge itself is the state. Grasp why this refutes many lengthy discussions.
The error comes from focusing on linguistic derivation rather than meaning.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider why Ghazali insists that 'alim' and 'ilm' refer to the same reality. This safeguards the unity of God's attributes without multiplying entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا تفصيل مذاهب الضلال، والغرضُ إثبات الصفات ، والبرهان القاطع : هو أن من ساعد على أن الله تعالى عالم . . فقد ساعد على أن له علماً ؛ فإن المفهوم من قولنا : عالم وله علمٌ . . واحد ؛ فإن العاقل يعقل زيادة الصفات دون ذاتاً ، ويعقلُها على حالة وصفة بعد ذلك ، فيكون قد عقل صفة وموصوفاً ، تغاير عن الذات والصفة علم مثلاً ، وله عبارتان : إحداهما طويلة ؛ وهي أن نقول : هذه الذات قد قام بها علم والأخرى وجيزة ، أو جزت بالتصريف والاشتقاق ؛ وهي أن الذات عالمة كما يشاهد الإنسانُ شخصاً ويشاهد نعلاً ويشاهد دخول رجله في النعل ، فله عبارة طويلة ؛ وهي أن يقول : هذا الشخص رجله داخلةٌ في نعله ، أو يقول : هو منتعل ، فلا معنى لكونه منتعلاً إلا أنه ذو نعل وما يُظَنُّ من أن قيام العلم بالذات يوجب للذات حالة تسمى عالمية .. هوس محض ، بل العلم هو الحالة (۱) ، فلا معنى لكونه عالماً إلا كونُ الذات على صفة وحال ، تيك الصفةُ والحال هي العلم فقط ، ولكن مَنْ يأخذُ المعاني من الألفاظ ؛ فإذا تكررت الألفاظ بالاشتقاقات .. لا بدَّ أن يغلط ؛ فاشتقاق صفة العالم من لفظ العلم أورثَ هذا الغلط ، فلا ينبغي أن يغتر به ، وبهذا يبطل جميع ما قيل وطُوِّل من العلة والمعلول ، وبطلان ذلك جلي بأوَّلِ العقل لمن لم يتكرز على سمعه ترديد تلك الألفاظ ، ومن عَلِقَ ذلك بفهمه.. فلا يمكن نزوعه منه إلا بكلام طويل لا يحتمله هذا المختصر .
So this is the detailed account of the doctrines of error. The purpose is to establish the attributes. The decisive proof is: whoever acknowledges that God Almighty is 'alim (knowing) has acknowledged that He has 'ilm (knowledge). For the concept of our saying 'alim and 'he has knowledge' is one. For the rational person conceives the increase of attributes without a (separate) essence, and conceives them upon a state and description after that; so he has conceived an attribute and a described subject, distinct from the essence. And the attribute, for example, knowledge, has two expressions: one lengthy: that we say 'This essence has knowledge subsisting in it'; and the other concise, or shortened by conjugation and derivation: that the essence is 'alima (knowing). Just as one observes a person and observes a sandal and observes the entry of his foot into the sandal; he has a lengthy expression: he says 'This person's foot is inside his sandal', or he says 'He is wearing a sandal'; so there is no meaning to his being 'wearing a sandal' except that he has a sandal. And what is thought, that the subsistence of knowledge in the essence entails for the essence a state called 'worldliness' (being knowing) is pure nonsense; rather, knowledge itself is the state. So there is no meaning to his being 'alim except that the essence is upon a description and a state; that description and state is only knowledge. But whoever takes meanings from words, when words are repeated by derivations, inevitably errs. So the derivation of the attribute 'alim from the word 'ilm has produced this error; one should not be deluded by it. And by this, all that has been said and elaborated about cause and effect is invalidated; and its invalidity is clear from the first of reason to one whose hearing has not become clogged by the repetition of those words. And whoever has become attached to that in his understanding cannot be weaned from it except by lengthy speech which this abridgment cannot contain.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is the decisive proof for the reality of attributes. Ghazali states that the purpose of the discussion is to prove that God has attributes distinct from His essence. The decisive proof: anyone who affirms that God is 'alim (knowing) has already affirmed that He has 'ilm (knowledge). The concept of 'alim and 'having knowledge' is identical. A rational person can conceive of an attribute added to the essence without a separate essence; he conceives an attribute and a described subject. Knowledge as an attribute can be expressed in two ways: a long form 'this essence has knowledge' and a short form 'the essence is knowing' (via derivation). This is like saying 'his foot is in the sandal' and 'he is sandaled'; both convey the same meaning. Ghazali then refutes the idea that the subsistence of knowledge in the essence creates a state called 'worldliness' ('alimiyya) separate from knowledge; rather, the knowledge itself is the state. The error arises from focusing on linguistic derivation: because the attribute 'alim is derived from 'ilm, some mistakenly think 'alim is something other than the presence of knowledge. Ghazali says this error invalidates much lengthy discussion about cause and effect. Reason alone shows the invalidity, but those habituated to verbal formulas may need longer refutation.

Misconception: That 'alim implies a separate state or quality (alimiyya) distinct from knowledge itself.
Correction: Ghazali argues that 'alim simply means the essence has knowledge; there is no third entity. Knowledge itself is the state.

Key Takeaway
Affirming that God knows necessarily affirms that He has knowledge, and no third 'state' is needed.

The Argument that 'Knowing' Adds to 'Existing'
Ghazali argues that 'alim' and 'mawjud' are not synonymous; 'alim' adds something.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's dialectical proof that the attribute of knowledge is a real addition to existence, not a mere synonym.

Core Idea
If 'knowing' meant the same as 'existing', calling God 'existing knowing' would be a meaningless tautology.

In this passage, Ghazali engages the philosophers and Mu'tazila who deny that attributes are distinct from the essence. He presents a concise dilemma: either 'alim' and 'mawjud' are identical, leading to an absurd tautology, or there is an increase. If there is an increase, it must be specific to the essence – and that increase is exactly what Muslims call knowledge. The argument forces the opponent to admit the reality of the attribute, even if they prefer different terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice how Ghazali structures the argument as a series of questions.
The passage is dense; take time to grasp each step.
2
Identify the dilemma
Ghazali poses two alternatives: either 'alim' means the same as 'mawjud' or it adds something. Identify each horn and its consequence.
The first horn leads to tautology, which is impossible for God.
3
Follow the second horn
If there is an increase, it must be specific to the essence. Otherwise it wouldn't be a description of God. What is that increase? Ghazali says it is knowledge.
Opponents end up agreeing on the reality, differing only in words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل : هو أنَّا نقول للفلاسفة والمعتزلة : هل المفهوم من قولنا : (عالم) عين المفهوم من قولنا : (موجود)، أو فيه إشارة إلى وجود وزيادة ؟ فإن قالوا : لا ؛ فإذن كان من قال : هو موجود عالم . . كأنه قال : هو موجود موجود ، وهذا ظاهرُ الاستحالة ، وإذا كان في مفهومه زيادة . . فتلك الزيادة : هل هي مختصة بذات الموجود أم لا ؟ فإن قالوا : لا محال ؛ إذ يخرج به عن أن يكون وصفاً له ، وإن كان مختصاً بذاته . . فنحنُ لا نعني بالعلم إلا ذاك ، وهي الزيادة المختصة بالذات الموجودة الزائدة على الوجود التي يحسن أن يشتق للموجود بسببه منه اسم العالم ، فقد ساعدتم على المعنى وعاد النزاع إلى اللفظ
The conclusion is: we say to the philosophers and Mu'tazila: Is the concept of our saying 'alim (knowing) identical to the concept of our saying 'mawjud (existing)', or does it refer to existence and an increase? If they say no, then one who says 'He is existing, knowing' would be like saying 'He is existing, existing', and this is evidently impossible. And if there is an increase in its concept, then that increase: is it specific to the essence of the existent or not? If they say no, impossible, for it would then cease to be a description of Him. And if it is specific to His essence, then we mean by knowledge nothing but that, namely the increase specific to the existing essence, additional to existence, on account of which it is proper to derive for the existent the name 'alim from it. So you have agreed to the meaning, and the dispute returns to the wording.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument against the philosophers and Mu'tazila who deny the reality of attributes. Ghazali asks: is the concept of 'alim (knowing) the same as 'mawjud (existing)' or does it involve an addition? If they say they are identical, then saying 'He is existing, knowing' is like 'He is existing, existing' which is impossible (tautology). So there must be an increase. Then the increase must be specific to the essence, otherwise it wouldn't be a description of God. If they admit it is specific to the essence, then that increase is exactly what we call 'ilm (knowledge). Thus, they have conceded the real point and only disagree about terminology (whether to call it 'attribute' or not). This is a powerful refutation showing that the opponents cannot avoid affirming a real distinction even if they deny the term.

Key Terms

المعتزلة
الوجود
الزيادة
العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali is merely playing word games, not proving a real distinction.
Correction: Ghazali uses logical necessity: if 'alim' were identical to 'mawjud', then the phrase would be tautologous, which is impossible for God. Thus a real increase is required, and that increase is the attribute of knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) It would imply God is not eternal
B) It would make 'He is existing, knowing' a tautology
C) It would deny God's power
D) It would mean God's essence is complex

Key Takeaway
Ghazali forces the opponent to admit that 'knowing' adds a real, essence-specific attribute, reducing the dispute to mere terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Logical Argument: Are Divine Attributes Surplus to Existence?
Ghazali presents a logical proof that the attribute of knowledge is a surplus beyond existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical argument that the concepts of 'knowing' and 'powerful' must add something to 'existent', thus affirming real distinct attributes.

Core Idea
If 'knowing' meant the same as 'existent', saying 'God is existent knowing' would be nonsense.

Ghazali engages philosophers and Mu'tazila who deny distinct divine attributes. He uses a simple logical dilemma: either the attribute term adds nothing to existence (leading to absurd repetition) or it adds something (which is the real attribute). He then shows that any denial of distinct attributes reduces to a verbal dispute. This passage is a masterclass in logical precision in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English translation. Note the key terms: mafhum, alim, mawjud, ziyada.
The passage is dense but logically structured.
2
Identify the central dilemma
Ghazali asks: Is the concept of 'alim' identical to that of 'mawjud' or does it indicate an increase? Think about the implications of each answer.
3
Analyze the first horn (identity)
If identical, then 'He is existent knowing' becomes 'He is existent existent', which is clearly absurd. This refutes the first option.
This shows that the concept of knowing cannot be exactly the same as existence.
4
Analyze the second horn (surplus)
If there is an increase, that increase must be specific to the essence, otherwise it wouldn't describe Him. That specific increase is exactly what we mean by knowledge as a real attribute.
Thus the opponent admits the meaning of a real attribute, and any remaining dispute is about words.
5
Apply the same logic to power
Ghazali then repeats the argument for 'qadir' compared to 'alim'. Either it's the same concept (making 'knowing and powerful' a tautology) or different (affirming two distinct concepts: knowledge and power).
This reinforces the necessity of multiple distinct attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل : هو أنَّا نقول للفلاسفة والمعتزلة : هل المفهوم من قولنا : (عالم) عين المفهوم من قولنا : (موجود)، أو فيه إشارة إلى وجود وزيادة ؟ فإن قالوا : لا ؛ فإذن كان من قال : هو موجود عالم . . كأنه قال : هو موجود موجود ، وهذا ظاهرُ الاستحالة ، وإذا كان في مفهومه زيادة . . فتلك الزيادة : هل هي مختصة بذات الموجود أم لا ؟ فإن قالوا : لا محال ؛ إذ يخرج به عن أن يكون وصفاً له ، وإن كان مختصاً بذاته . . فنحنُ لا نعني بالعلم إلا ذاك ، وهي الزيادة المختصة بالذات الموجودة الزائدة على الوجود التي يحسن أن يشتق للموجود بسببه منه اسم العالم ، فقد ساعدتم على المعنى وعاد النزاع إلى اللفظ . وإن أردت إيراده على الفلاسفة .. قلت : مفهوم قولنا : ( قادر ) هو مفهوم قولنا : (عالم) أم غيره (۲) ؟ فإن كان ذلك هو بعينه .. فكأنا قلنا : قادر قادر ، فإنه تكرار محض وإن كان غيره .. فإذن هو المراد ، فقد أثبتم مفهومين : أحدهما بالقدرة ، والآخر بالعلم ، ورجع الإنكار إلى اللفظ
The upshot: We say to the philosophers (falasifa) and the Mu'tazila: Is the concept (mafhum) of our statement 'knowing' (alim) identical to the concept of our statement 'existent' (mawjud), or does it indicate existence and an increase? If they say 'No', then when one says 'He is existent knowing', it is as if he said 'He is existent existent', and this is clearly impossible. And if in its concept there is an increase, then that increase: is it specific to the essence of the existent or not? If they say 'No', it is impossible, for then it would cease to be a description of Him. And if it is specific to His essence, then we do not mean by knowledge (ilm) except that – the increase specific to the existent essence, additional to existence, by reason of which the name 'knowing' is properly derived for the existent. So you have agreed on the meaning and the dispute returns to the word. And if you wish to present it to the philosophers, you say: Is the concept of our statement 'powerful' (qadir) identical to the concept of our statement 'knowing' (alim) or other than it? If it is the very same, then we have said 'powerful powerful', which is mere repetition. And if it is other than it, then that is what is intended. So you have affirmed two concepts: one by power (qudra) and the other by knowledge (ilm), and the denial returns to the word.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof (burhan) addressed to philosophers and Mu'tazila who deny distinct attributes. Ghazali states the upshot (al-hasil) of his argument. He poses a dilemma: In the statement 'He is knowing (alim)', is the concept (mafhum) identical to 'existent (mawjud)' or does it add something? If identical, then 'He is existent knowing' would be redundant like 'existent existent', which is absurd. If it adds something, then that addition (ziyada) is either specific to the essence of the existent or not. If not specific, it cannot be a real attribute of Him. If specific, then that addition is exactly what we mean by 'ilm' (knowledge) – a real attribute over and above existence. Thus, the opponent has implicitly admitted the reality of the attribute, and any remaining disagreement is merely verbal (lafzi). Ghazali then applies the same argument to power (qudra): The concept of 'qadir' compared to 'alim' must be either identical or different. If identical, calling God both 'alim' and 'qadir' is a tautology. If different, then two distinct concepts (knowledge and power) are affirmed, and again the denial is only verbal. This shows that the opponents unwittingly affirm distinct attributes.

Key Terms
المفهوم (al-mafhum, concept)
العالم (al-alim, the Knowing)
الموجود (al-mawjud, the Existent)
الزيادة (al-ziyada, increase/surplus)
القدرة (al-qudra, power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali's argument is only about words and does not prove real attributes.
Correction: Ghazali shows that whatever the opponent calls the increase, it is a real attribute beyond existence; the dispute becomes verbal only after both sides agree on the reality of the attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, if the concept of 'alim' is identical to 'mawjud', what follows?
A) God has no attributes.
B) Saying 'God is existent knowing' is like saying 'God is existent existent'.
C) Knowledge is not a real attribute.
D) The dispute is merely verbal.

Key Takeaway
The logical argument affirms that divine attributes like knowledge and power are real increases over mere existence, not just synonyms for existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Knowing,' do you truly believe that He knows you completely and inescapably, or is it just a phrase you repeat? How does Ghazali's logical proof challenge you to treat Allah's knowledge as a real, additional reality that demands accountability in your daily choices? What will you do today to live as if His knowledge is a real presence watching over your actions?
?
If denying the reality of attributes leads to logical absurdity (like saying 'existent existent'), are there areas of your faith where you have made Allah's attributes empty names—for example, trusting in His mercy without letting His justice affect how you treat others? How will you correct that starting now?
?
Ghazali shows that even those who deny attributes verbally end up affirming them implicitly when they distinguish between knowledge and power.
