The Multiplicity Objection and Ghazali's Honest Response
Ghazali responds to an objection that his view leads to infinite attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the objection against attribute multiplicity and why Ghazali treats it with profound seriousness rather than dismissing it.

Core Idea
Honest theology admits its hardest questions.

This passage shows Ghazali facing a powerful objection: if God's attributes are distinct concepts, why stop at a finite number? The objector pushes the logic to its extreme, threatening to collapse into infinite attributes or a reductionist monism. Ghazali's response is not a quick refutation but a candid admission that this is a great problem requiring deeper investigation. Let's work through the objection and his reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the structure: objection (fa-in qila) followed by answer (al-jawab).
The passage is incomplete due to page break; Ghazali's answer is only the beginning.
2
Analyze the objection's two prongs
The objector applies the same dilemma to two examples: (1) command vs. prohibition, (2) knowledge of accidents vs. substances. In each, whether the concepts are identical or different leads to an absurdity.
Identify the two horns of the dilemma in each case.
3
Trace the slippery slope
The objector generalizes: if one attribute can cover different objects, why not reduce all attributes to one, or the essence alone? That would yield Mu'tazilism.
Note how the objector forces a choice between multiplicity and reduction.
4
Reflect on Ghazali's answer
Ghazali admits the objection is a 'great axis' of the problems of attributes, beyond the scope of this work. He only alludes to the beginning of the solution, noting that most investigators have recoiled from it.
This shows intellectual honesty, not evasion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قولكم : (آمرٌ) مفهومُهُ عينُ المفهوم من قولكم : (ناه) أو غيره ؟ فإن كان عينه .. فهو تكرار محض ، وإن كان غيره.. فليكن له كلام هو أمرٌ ، وآخرُ هو نهي ، وآخرُ هو خبر ، وليكن خطاب كل نبي مفارقاً لخطاب غيره ! وكذلك مفهوم قولكم : إنه عالم بالأعراض ؛ أهو عين مفهوم قولكم : ( إنه عالم بالجواهر) أو غيره ؟ فإن كان عينه .. فليكن الإنسان العالم بالجوهر عالماً بالعرض بعين ذلك العلم ؛ حتى يتعلق علم واحد بمتعلقات كثيرة لا نهاية لها ، وإن كان غيرَه .. فليكن الله تعالى علوم مختلفة لا نهاية لها ، وكذا الكلام والقدرة والإرادة ، وكلُّ صفة لا نهاية لمتعلقاتها ينبغي يكون لأعداد تلك الصفة نهاية ، وهذا محال . فإن جاز أن تكون صفةٌ واحدةٌ تكون هي الأمر وهي النهي وهي الخبر ، وتنوب عن هذه المختلفات .. جاز أن تكون صفةٌ واحدة تنوب عن العلم ولم لا تكون الذات والقدرة والحياة وسائر الصفات ، ثم إذا جاز ذلك .. جازَ أن تكون الذاتُ وحدها كافية عن الصفات ؟ وعند ذلك يلزم مذهب المعتزلة والفلاسفة ؟ والجواب : أن نقول : هذا السؤال يحرّك قطباً عظيماً من إشكالات الصفات ، ولا يليقُ حلُّها بالمختصرات ، ولكن إذا سبق القلم إلى إيراده .. فلترمز إلى مبدأ الطريق في حلّهِ ، وقد كاعَ عنه أكثر المحصلين(۱) ، وعدلوا
For if it is said: Your statement 'commanding' (amir) – is its concept identical to the concept of your statement 'prohibiting' (nahi) or other than it? If it is identical, then it is pure repetition; if it is other, then let Him have speech which is a command, another which is a prohibition, and another which is a report, and let the speech of each prophet be distinct from the speech of another! And likewise, the concept of your statement that He knows accidents (a'rad) – is it identical to the concept of your statement that He knows substances (jawahir) or other? If it is identical, then let a human who knows a substance know an accident with that same knowledge, so that a single knowledge would relate to infinitely many objects. If it is other, then let God have infinitely many different knowledges. And similarly for speech, power, will, and every attribute whose objects are infinite – the number of those attributes would have to be infinite, and this is impossible. For if it is permissible that a single attribute be the command, the prohibition, and the report, and stand in for these different things, then it is permissible that a single attribute stand in for knowledge. Why not the essence, power, life, and the rest of the attributes? Then if that is permissible, it would be permissible for the essence alone to suffice for the attributes. And then the doctrine of the Mu'tazila and philosophers would follow. The answer: We say: This question moves a great axis of the problems of the attributes, and their solution is not suitable for brief works. But since the pen has preceded to its mention, let us allude to the beginning of the path in solving it. Most of the investigators have recoiled from it and turned away.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an objection (fa-in qila) followed by an answer (al-jawab). The objector uses Ghazali's own method against him. If the concepts of 'commanding' and 'prohibiting' are different, then God must have multiple distinct instances of speech (kalam) – one for each type, and even each prophet gets distinct speech, leading to multiplicity. Similarly, if knowing accidents and knowing substances are different concepts, then either one knowledge knows both (which is impossible for human parallel), or God has infinite knowledges. Following this logic, if a single attribute can cover multiple objects (e.g., one speech can be command, prohibition, report), then why not reduce all attributes to one, or the essence alone? This would collapse into Mu'tazilism and philosophy (denial of attributes). In the answer, Ghazali acknowledges the profundity of this objection – it touches a major axis (qutb) of the problems of attributes. He confesses that a full solution is beyond the scope of this brief work. He only alludes to the beginning of the path: most investigators (muhassilun) have recoiled from it. This shows Ghazali's awareness of the difficulty and his restraint in not oversimplifying.

Key Terms
الآمر (al-amir, the Commanding)
الناهي (al-nahi, the Prohibiting)
الأعراض (al-a'rad, accidents)
الجواهر (al-jawahir, substances)
المتعلقات (al-muta'alliqat, objects of attributes)
القطب (al-qutb, the axis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali dismisses the objection as invalid or easily answerable.
Correction: Ghazali actually calls it a 'great axis' of problems and admits most scholars have recoiled from it, showing deep respect for the difficulty.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali respond to the objection that his view leads to multiple attributes?
A) He provides a complete refutation.
B) He acknowledges the difficulty and alludes to the beginning of a solution.
C) He rejects the objection as invalid.
D) He changes his position to that of the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali treats a difficult objection with intellectual honesty, admitting its profundity rather than offering a simplistic rebuttal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological difficulty that you cannot resolve, do you admit your limitation or pretend to have an answer? How does Ghazali's example challenge your intellectual honesty before God?
?
The objection suggests that God's attributes might collapse into His essence, reducing Him to a bare unity. Does your belief in God's attributes make your worship more intimate or more abstract? What would you lose if you could not affirm real attributes?
?
Ghazali says most scholars 'recoiled' from this problem. Are you willing to sit with uncomfortable questions about God, or do you prefer easy answers? What would it cost you to embrace the difficulty of theology?

Divine Attributes

The Identity-Distinctness Dilemma for Divine Attributes
Ghazali presents the objection that divine attributes either duplicate or multiply infinitely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that proponents of multiple divine attributes face: either the attributes are identical (leading to redundancy) or distinct (leading to infinite multiplication).

Core Idea
Divine attributes must be neither identical nor distinct—this is the central dilemma of Ash'ari theology.

Ghazali presents a powerful objection against those who affirm multiple real attributes of God. The objection forces a choice: either 'powerful' and 'knowing' mean the same thing (making the phrase 'God is powerful and knowing' a tautology), or they mean different things (implying that God has distinct attributes for every function, leading to infinite attributes). This dilemma, applied to speech and knowledge, threatens the coherence of the attribute doctrine. In this lesson, you will follow Ghazali's logical reductio.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic dilemma
Notice how the objector first frames the problem: if 'powerful' and 'knowing' refer to the same concept, then saying both is pure repetition; if they refer to different concepts, then two distinct entities are affirmed. This sets up the dilemma.
The dilemma is also applied to divine speech (command, prohibition, information).
2
Apply the dilemma to speech
Read the part about speech: if 'commander' and 'forbidder' have the same meaning, it is repetition; if different, then God must have separate acts of speech for each command, prohibition, and information, leading to a multiplicity of speeches.
The objector suggests this would require distinct addresses to different prophets.
3
Apply the dilemma to knowledge
Now consider knowledge of accidents vs. substances: if identical, then one who knows a substance automatically knows all accidents, which is false; if different, then God would have infinite sciences for infinite objects of knowledge.
This would also apply to other attributes with infinite objects.
4
Grasp the counter-argument
The objector turns the tables: if one attribute (e.g., speech) can serve multiple functions (command, prohibition, information), why not one attribute for all? This would imply that the divine essence alone suffices, leading to the Mu'tazilite and philosopher position.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the multiple-attributes position.
5
Reflect on the challenge
Consider how a defender of attributes might respond. The objection does not disprove attributes but forces a choice between redundancy and infinite regress. The Ash'ari must find a middle way, such as asserting that attributes are really distinct but not separate entities.
This lesson does not provide the Ash'ari response; it only presents the objection.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان ذلك هو بعينه .. فكأنا قلنا : قادر قادر ، فإنه تكرار محض
وإن كان غيره .. فإذن هو المراد ، فقد أثبتم مفهومين : أحدهما بالقدرة ، والآخر بالعلم ، ورجع الإنكار إلى اللفظ
عنه يعبر
تحريجة فعلى فإن قيل : قولكم : (آمرٌ) مفهومُهُ عينُ المفهوم من قولكم : (ناه[؟]) مسلككم هذا يجب نفي الصفات لوجود[؟] ومخبرٌ أو غيره ؟ فإن كان عينه .. فهو تكرار محض ، وإن كان غيره..
فليكن له كلام هو أمرٌ ، وآخرُ هو نهي ، وآخرُ هو خبر ، وليكن خطاب كل متعلقاتها نبي مفارقاً لخطاب غيره !
وكذلك مفهوم قولكم : إنه عالم بالأعراض ؛ أهو عين مفهوم قولكم : ( إنه عالم بالجواهر) أو غيره ؟ فإن كان عينه .. فليكن الإنسان العالم بالجوهر عالماً بالعرض بعين ذلك العلم ؛ حتى يتعلق علم واحد بمتعلقات كثيرة لا نهاية لها ، وإن كان غيرَه .. فليكن الله تعالى علوم مختلفة لا نهاية لها ، وكذا الكلام والقدرة والإرادة ، وكلُّ صفة لا نهاية لمتعلقاتها ينبغي يكون لأعداد تلك الصفة نهاية ، وهذا محال
فإن جاز أن تكون صفةٌ واحدةٌ تكون هي الأمر وهي النهي وهي الخبر ، وتنوب عن هذه المختلفات .. جاز أن تكون صفةٌ واحدة تنوب عن العلم
ولم لا تكون الذات والقدرة والحياة وسائر الصفات ، ثم إذا جاز ذلك .. جازَ أن تكون الذاتُ وحدها كافية عن بنفسها كافية ، ويكون فيها معنى القدرة والعلم وسائر الصفات من غير زيادة ، وعند ذلك يلزم مذهب المعتزلة والفلاسفة ؟
If that is the very same thing, then it is as if we said: 'powerful, powerful', for it is pure repetition. And if it is something else, then that is the intended meaning; you have affirmed two concepts: one by power, and the other by knowledge, and the denial returns to the wording. ... So if it is said: your saying: 'commander' its concept is the same as the concept of your saying: 'forbidden' while your course of action requires denial of attributes for existence, and 'informer' or something else? If it is the same, it is pure repetition; if it is something else... Then let there be for Him speech that is command, and another that is prohibition, and another that is information, and let the address of each of its objects be a prophet distinct from the address of another! And likewise the concept of your saying: He knows accidents; is it the same as the concept of your saying: He knows substances or something else? If it is the same, then let the human who knows substance know accident by that same knowledge, so that a single knowledge relates to many infinite objects; and if it is something else, then let God have different infinite sciences, and likewise speech, power, and will, and every attribute whose objects are infinite should have a finite number for that attribute, and this is impossible. For if it is permissible that one attribute be the command, the prohibition, and the information, and substitute for these different things, then it is permissible that one attribute substitute for knowledge. And why should not the essence, power, life, and the rest of the attributes be such? Then if that is permissible, it is permissible that the essence alone be sufficient by itself, and that there be in it the meaning of power, knowledge, and the rest of the attributes without increase, and thereby the doctrine of the Mu'tazila and the philosophers follows?
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the common Ash'ari position on divine attributes. It begins with a hypothetical: if the attribute 'powerful' and the attribute 'knowing' are identical in meaning, then saying 'God is powerful and knowing' is mere repetition like saying 'powerful, powerful'. If they are different, then the phrase 'He is powerful' and 'He is knowing' refer to two distinct concepts, and the objector's denial merely concerns the wording. The objection then extends to the question of God's speech: if 'command', 'prohibition', and 'information' have the same concept, they are redundant; if different, then God has separate speeches for each, and each speech addresses its object (like a prophet) separately. Similarly, regarding knowledge: if knowledge of accidents is identical to knowledge of substances, then a human who knows one should automatically know the other, which is false; if different, then God would have infinite sciences since there are infinite objects. This is absurd. The objector then turns the argument: if one attribute (like a single 'speech') can serve multiple functions (command, prohibition, information), why not one attribute for all? This would lead to the view that the divine essence alone suffices for all attributes, which is the position of the Mu'tazila and philosophers. The objection thus forces the defender of multiple attributes to choose between redundancy and infinite regress.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a rejection of divine attributes altogether.
Correction: The objection is not a rejection but a logical dilemma forcing a choice between redundancy and infinite regress. The Ash'ari must find a middle way, e.g., that attributes are really distinct but not separate entities.

Checkpoint

B) This leads to the Mu'tazilite and philosopher view that the essence alone suffices.
C) This shows that speech and knowledge are identical.
D) This confirms the Ash'ari position on attributes.

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the coherence of multiple divine attributes, forcing a choice between redundancy and infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray to 'The All-Powerful, The All-Knowing', do you ever pause to check if you are mentally attributing two separate 'things' to God? How does this dilemma make you re-examine the way you think about God's attributes?
?
If this logical dilemma shows that human reason cannot fully harmonize multiple divine attributes without falling into absurdity, are you willing to accept a degree of mystery in your faith rather than forcing a neat logical system? What will you do with the tension this creates?
?
This passage uses a reductio ad absurdum to challenge a theological position. Have you ever held a belief about God without thinking through its logical implications? Would you be willing to abandon or refine that belief if it leads to an absurdity?

The Three Positions on Divine Attributes
Ghazali outlines two extreme views and the moderate middle path regarding God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of the three major positions on divine attributes—two extremes and a middle path—and why the middle path is considered the most sound.

Core Idea
The middle path avoids both collapsing attributes into essence and multiplying them infinitely.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a profound question about God's attributes. He notes that earlier scholars simply appealed to revelation, but he finds that insufficient because revelation itself distinguishes between different items. Instead, he introduces a rational framework: every rational person recognizes that evidence points to something beyond the mere essence of the Creator—attributes like knowledge and power. The various schools of thought then fall into three categories: two extremes and a middle path. This lesson unpacks those categories and clarifies why the middle path is the most balanced and correct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice Ghazali's style: he starts by acknowledging the difficulty, then critiques the purely scriptural approach, and finally presents the three positions.
The passage is incomplete at the end, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the two extremes
Identify the extreme of laxity (tafrīṭ) attributed to the philosophers, and the extreme of excess (ifrāṭ) attributed to some Mu'tazila and Karāmiyya. Understand what each extreme claims about attributes.
The philosophers reduce all attributes to a single essence; the other extreme multiplies attributes infinitely.
3
Understand the middle path
Focus on the third view: the middle path. It distinguishes between things that differ in essence (e.g., power vs. knowledge) and things that differ only in relation (e.g., objects of knowledge). This avoids both extremes.
The middle path is not a compromise but a principled distinction.
4
Reflect on Ghazali's critique
Consider why Ghazali rejects the purely scriptural approach. He argues that revelation itself implies multiplicity (e.g., command vs. prohibition), so one cannot simply stop at affirming one attribute.
This motivates the need for rational analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن نقول : هذا السؤال يحرّك قطباً عظيماً من إشكالات الصفات ، ولا يليقُ حلُّها بالمختصرات ، ولكن إذا سبق القلم إلى إيراده .. فلترمز إلى مبدأ الطريق في حلّهِ ، وقد كاعَ عنه أكثر المحصلين(۱) ، وعدلوا
إلى التمسك بالكتاب والإجماع ، وقالوا : هذه الصفات قد ورد الشرعُ بها ؛ إذ دلَّ الشرعُ على العلم ، وفُهِمَ منه الواحد لا محالة ، والزائد على الواحد لم يرد . . فلا نعتقده
وهذا يكاد لا يشفي ؛ فإنه قد ورد بالأمر والنهي والخبر ، والتوراة والإنجيل والقرآن ، فما المانع من أن يقال : الأمر غيرُ النهي ، والقرآنُ غيرُ التوراة ؟ وقد ورد بأنه تعالى يعلم السر والعلانية ، والظاهر والباطن والرطب واليابس ، وهلم جراً إلى ما يشتمل القرآن عليه ؟
فلعل الجوابَ ما نشير إلى مطلع تحقيقه ؛ وهو أن كل فريق من العقلاء مضطر إلى أن يعترف بأن الدليل قد دلَّ على أمر زائد على وجودِ ذاتِ الصانع ، وهو الذي يعبَّرُ عنه بأنه عالمٌ وقادرٌ وغيره ، والاحتمالات فيه مذاهب الفرق في ثلاثة : طرفان وواسطة ، والاقتصاد أقرب إلى السداد
أما الطرفان .. فأحدهما في التفريط : وهو الاقتصار على ذات واحدة تؤدي جميع هذه المعاني ، وتنوب عنها كما قالت الفلاسفة
والثاني طرف الإفراط : وهو إثبات صفة لا نهاية لأحادها من العلوم والقدر والكلام ، وذلك بحسب عدد متعلقاتِ هذه الصفات ، وهذا إسرافٌ لا صائرَ إليه إلا بعضُ المعتزلة وبعض الكرامية .
والرأي الثالث هو القصد والوسط : وهو أن يقال المختلفات لاختلافها درجات في التقارب والتباعد ، فرُبَّ شيئين يختلفان بذاتيهما كاختلاف الحركة والسكون ، واختلاف القدرة والعلم ، والجوهر والعرض ، وربَّ شيئين يدخلان تحت حَدٌ وحقيقة واحدة ، لا يختلفان لذاتيهما ، وإنما يكونُ
And the answer: we say: This question stirs a great pivot of problems concerning the attributes, and their solution is not fitting for brief works, but if the pen has preceded to its mention, then let us point to the beginning of the path in its solution. Most of the investigators have recoiled from it and turned to clinging to the Book and consensus, saying: These attributes have come in the Law; since the Law indicated knowledge, and from it the one is understood necessarily, and what is additional to the one has not come, so we do not believe it. But this hardly suffices, for commands, prohibitions, and information have come, and the Torah, the Gospel, and the Qur'an—what prevents one from saying that command is other than prohibition, and the Qur'an other than the Torah? And it has come that He knows the secret and the open, the apparent and the hidden, the moist and the dry, and so on to what the Qur'an contains. So perhaps the answer is what we point to the beginning of its verification: that every group of rational people is compelled to acknowledge that the evidence has indicated something additional to the existence of the Creator's essence, which is expressed by His being knowing, powerful, etc. The possibilities concerning it are the schools of the sects in three: two extremes and a middle, and moderation is closer to correctness. As for the two extremes: one of them is the extreme of laxity: restricting to a single essence that performs all these meanings and substitutes for them, as the philosophers said. The second is the extreme of excess: affirming attributes of which the individuals are infinite, such as sciences, powers, and speeches, according to the number of objects of these attributes—this is extravagance followed only by some Mu'tazila and some Karamiyya. The third view is the purpose and the middle: that it is said that different things have degrees in proximity and distance, so perhaps two things differ in their essences like the difference between motion and rest, and the difference between power and knowledge, and substance and accident; and perhaps two things fall under a single limit and reality, not differing in their essences, but only [differ] in...
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the previous objection. Ghazali begins by acknowledging that the question is a major crux of attribute problems, not easily resolved in a short work. He notes that most earlier scholars avoided rational investigation and simply appealed to revelation and consensus, arguing that only what is revealed (e.g., knowledge) should be affirmed, and nothing extra. But he finds this insufficient because revelation itself mentions multiple distinct items (commands, prohibitions, books, and various objects of knowledge). So a better answer is needed. He then introduces the key: evidence necessitates something beyond the mere existence of the Creator—attributes like knowledge and power. Regarding these attributes, there are three possibilities: (1) The extreme of laxity (al-tafrit): reducing everything to a single essence that performs all functions, as the philosophers held. (2) The extreme of excess (al-ifrat): affirming infinite individual attributes (e.g., infinite knowledges, powers, speeches) corresponding to every object, which only some Mu'tazila and Karamiyya adopted. (3) The middle path (al-qasd wa al-wasat): recognizing that different things have varying degrees of nearness and distance. Some things differ in essence (e.g., motion vs. rest, power vs. knowledge, substance vs. accident), while others fall under a single reality and differ only in relation (e.g., different objects of knowledge do not multiply knowledge itself). This middle view avoids both extremes: attributes are neither collapsed into one nor multiplied infinitely. The passage ends abruptly, but the intended conclusion is that knowledge of diverse objects does not require diverse knowledges, as they are related to the same attribute differing only by relation.

Key Terms
تفريط
إفراط
وسط
اقتصاد
فلاسفة
معتزلة
كرامية
قدرة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The middle path is just a compromise between two extremes, averaging out their claims.
Correction: The middle path is a principled distinction: some differences are essential (e.g., knowledge vs. power), others are merely relational (e.g., different objects of knowledge do not multiply knowledge). This avoids both monism and infinite pluralism.

Checkpoint
Which groups does Ghazali associate with the extreme of excess (الإفراط) in attributes?
A) The philosophers and the Ash'aris
B) Some Mu'tazila and some Karamiyya
C) The Jahmiyya and the Murji'a
D) The entire Mu'tazila and all Karamiyya

Key Takeaway
The middle path on divine attributes recognizes that some differences between attributes are essential (e.g., knowledge vs. power) while others are merely relational (e.g., objects of knowledge), thus avoiding both the extreme of collapsing all attributes into one and the extreme of multiplying them infinitely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which extreme—laxity (reducing God's attributes to nothing distinct) or excess (multiplying them endlessly)—do you find yourself drifting toward in your own thoughts? What shifts in your daily life and worship would guard against that drift?
?
Ghazali says that the middle path requires careful reasoning, not just clinging to texts. Are you willing to patiently study nuanced theological distinctions even when they challenge your comfort? If not, what might you be risking?
?
If you truly internalized that some of God's attributes differ essentially while others only relationally, how would that change the way you speak about God or pray to Him? Be specific: how would your words or heart be different tomorrow morning?

The Three Positions on Divine Attributes
Ghazali enumerates three positions on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of the three positions on divine attributes: the extreme of denial, the extreme of excess, and the balanced middle path.

Core Idea
Rational proof demands attributes beyond the essence; three schools emerge: denial, excess, and the middle.

Ghazali argues that mere scripture-based denial of attributes is insufficient because scripture itself contains diverse commands and reports. He then presents the three possible positions on divine attributes, warning against both negligence and excess, and pointing to the middle path as the correct one.

Identify the three positions

Why does Ghazali reject both extremes? Evaluate his reasoning.

وهذا يكاد لا يشفي؛ فإنه قد ورد بالأمر والنهي والخبر، والتوراة والإنجيل والقرآن، فما المانع من أن يقال: الأمر غير النهي، والقرآن غير التوراة؟ وقد ورد بأنه تعالى يعلم السر والعلانية، والظاهر والباطن والرطب واليابس، وهلم جراً إلى ما يشتمل القرآن عليه؟ العقلاء فلعل الجواب ما نشير إلى مطلع تحقيقه؛ وهو أن كل فريق مضطر إلى أن يعترف بأن الدليل قد دل على أمر زائد على وجود ذات الصانع، وهو الذي يعبر عنه بأنه عالم وقادر وغيره، والاحتمالات فيه مذاهب الفرق في ثلاثة: طرفان وواسطة، والاقتصاد أقرب إلى السداد. أما الطرفان: فأحدهما في التفريط: وهو الاقتصار على ذات واحدة تؤدي جميع هذه المعاني، وتنوب عنها كما قالت الفلاسفة (١). والثاني طرف الإفراط: وهو إثبات صفة لا نهاية لأحادها من العلوم والقدر والكلام، وذلك بحسب عدد متعلقات هذه الصفات، وهذا إسراف لا صائر إليه إلا بعض المعتزلة وبعض الكرامية. والرأي الثالث هو القصد والوسط: وهو أن يقال المختلفات لاختلافها درجات في التقارب والتباعد، فرب شيئين يختلفان بذاتيهما كاختلاف الحركة والسكون، واختلاف القدرة والعلم، والجوهر والعرض، ورب شيئين يدخلان تحت حد وحقيقة واحدة، لا يختلفان لذاتيهما، وإنما يكون الاختلاف فيهما من جهة تغاير التعلق، فليس الاختلاف بين القدرة والعلم كالاختلاف بين العلم بسواد والعلم بسواد آخر أو ببياض؛ ولذلك إذا حددت العلم بحد.. يدخل فيه العلم بالمعلومات كلها. فنقول: الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد أن يقال: كل اختلاف يرجع إلى تباين الذوات بأنفسها فلا يمكن أن يكفي الواحد منها وينوب عن المختلفات: فوجب أن يكون العلم غير القدرة، وكذا الصفات السبع، وأن تكون الصفات غير الذات من حيث إن المباينة بين الصفة والذات الموصوفة وبين أشد من المباينة بين الصفتين. وأما العلم بالشيء.. فلا يخالف العلم بغيره إلا من وجه تعلقه بالمتعلق، فلا يبعد أن تتميز الصفة القديمة بهذه الخاصية، وهو ألأ يوجب تباين المتعلقات فيها تبايناً وتعدداً.
This hardly suffices; for the [texts] have come with command and prohibition and report, and the Torah and the Gospel and the Qur'an. So what prevents one from saying: command is other than prohibition, and the Qur'an is other than the Torah? And it has come that He, exalted, knows the secret and the public, the manifest and the hidden, the moist and the dry, and so on to what the Qur'an contains? The rational people. Perhaps the answer is what we indicate toward the beginning of its verification: that every faction is compelled to admit that the proof has indicated an additional matter beyond the existence of the Essence of the Maker, which is expressed by His being knowing and powerful and other [attributes]. The possibilities concerning it are the schools of the sects in three: two extremes and a middle, and moderation is closer to correctness. As for the two extremes: one of them is in negligence: confining to a single essence that fulfills all these meanings and stands in for them as the philosophers said. And the second is the extreme of excess: affirming attributes no limit to their units of knowledge and power and speech, according to the number of the objects of these attributes, and this is extravagance which none adopt except some of the Mu'tazila and some of the Karramiyya. And the third opinion is the purpose and the middle: that one should say that the things differ in degrees of closeness and distance; perhaps two things differ in their essences like the difference between motion and rest, and the difference between power and knowledge, and substance and accident; and perhaps two things fall under one definition and one reality, not differing in their essences, but the difference between them is only in respect of the variation of relation. So the difference between power and knowledge is not like the difference between knowledge of black and knowledge of another black or of white; therefore, if you define knowledge by a definition, it includes knowledge of all objects of knowledge. So we say: moderation in belief is to say: every difference that goes back to the disparity of the essences themselves cannot be satisfied by one [thing] and stand in for the different things; so it is necessary that knowledge be other than power, and likewise the seven attributes, and that the attributes be other than the essence in that the distinction between the attribute and the described essence is greater than the distinction between the two attributes. As for knowledge of a thing, it does not differ from knowledge of another except in respect of its relation to the object of knowledge; so it is not unlikely that the eternal attribute is distinguished by this property, namely that disparity of objects does not necessitate disparity and multiplicity in it.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the three positions on divine attributes, followed by an explanation of the balanced middle path. Ghazali begins by critiquing the simplistic view of adhering only to what scripture explicitly mentions, showing that the very scriptures command, prohibit, and contain diverse reports, implying diversity in the attributes. He then states that rational proof compels us to affirm something beyond the bare existence of the Essence, i.e., the attributes. The schools are divided into three: (1) extreme denial (ta'til) as held by philosophers who collapse all attributes into the essence; (2) extreme affirmation (ghulu) as held by some Mu'tazila and Karramiyya who multiply attributes according to the number of objects; (3) the middle path of moderation. Ghazali explains that differences between things are of two types: those that differ in essence (like motion and rest, power and knowledge) and those that differ only in relation (like knowledge of one black object vs. another). For essences that differ, one cannot substitute one for another; therefore, power and knowledge are distinct attributes distinct from the essence itself. For knowledge, the difference is merely in relation to the object, so the attribute itself is not multiplied by the multiplicity of objects. This is the balanced belief (iqtisad) that Ghazali defends.

Key Terms
الصفات
التفريط
الإفراط
القصد والوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Scripture alone suffices to determine the nature of divine attributes.
Correction: Ghazali argues that scripture itself implies diversity, requiring rational proof to establish attributes beyond essence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which group holds the extreme of excess (إفراط)?
A) Philosophers
B) Some Mu'tazila and Karramiyya
C) All Mu'tazila
D) Ash'arites

Key Takeaway
The three positions on divine attributes are the extreme of denial (philosophers), the extreme of excess (some Mu'tazila/Karramiyya), and the balanced middle path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes, do you find yourself tending toward reducing them to concepts (like the philosophers) or multiplying them carelessly? Which extreme do you lean toward, and how does Ghazali's 'middle path' challenge you to reexamine that tendency?
?
If rational proof demands that attributes are real and distinct from God's essence, how does that affect your prayer and reliance on a God who knows you specifically, not vaguely? Will you today interact with Him as the All-Knowing, All-Powerful, All-Speaking Lord, or as an abstract force?
?
In your daily life, where else do you fall into extremes (in your words, actions, or beliefs) that mirror the extremes on divine attributes? Will you commit to seeking the balanced middle path—the 'purpose and the middle'—even when it is harder than taking a side?

The Balanced Middle Path Explained
Ghazali explains the rational basis for the middle path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's detailed argument for the middle path, distinguishing between essential and relational differences among attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes differ either in essence or in relation; only essential differences imply distinct attributes.

Having outlined the three positions, Ghazali now explains why the middle path is rationally necessary. He distinguishes two types of differences: those in the essences themselves and those only in relation to objects. This distinction allows him to affirm the seven attributes as distinct from the essence while denying infinite multiplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the key distinction.
2
Identify the two types of difference
Find where Ghazali talks about things differing in their essences vs. in relation.
Example: power vs. knowledge vs. knowledge of black vs. white.
3
Apply the distinction to attributes
How does this lead to the conclusion that knowledge and power are distinct, but knowledge is not multiplied by objects?
4
Understand the relationship between attributes and essence
Ghazali states that attributes are distinct from the essence, and the distinction between attributes is lesser than between attribute and essence.

Source Text

?

Commit to meditating on this distinction for one minute each day for the next week.
?
The passage says that knowledge of different objects does not make knowledge multiple; it is one attribute that relates to different objects. Reflect on your own knowledge: do you treat your knowledge of different things as separate possessions, or do you see it as one faculty that relates to many? How might this reflection help you understand Allah's knowledge more deeply? Write down one area where you can apply this insight to strengthen your awe of Allah's omniscience.
?
Ghazali describes the balanced belief (iqtisad) as steering between extremes. In your own life, are there areas of your faith where you tend to go to an extreme (e.g., over-rationalizing or being overly literal)? How can the principle of distinguishing between essential and relational differences guide you back to the middle path? Make a concrete plan to adjust one such area starting today.

Objection and Answer: Justifying the Middle Position
Ghazali addresses an objection that his middle position does not remove all doubt and defends it as sufficiently preferable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends his middle position against the objection that it does not eliminate all doubt, and why he considers residual doubt acceptable in matters of divine attributes.

Core Idea
A valid theological argument does not require removing all doubt, only showing clear preference over alternatives.

Ghazali anticipates a sharp objection to his middle path: if you admit that the problem of divine attributes still involves doubt, then what have you really solved? In his answer, he shifts the criteria of success from absolute certainty to decisive preferability. He argues that for transcendent matters, some level of doubt is inevitable, and the best we can do is adopt the position that minimizes it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection Ghazali presents. Notice how it challenges his entire approach by claiming the problem remains unsolved.
The objector assumes that a successful argument must cut off all doubt.
2
Analyze Ghazali's response strategy
Ghazali redefines the goal: the aim is to show decisive preference, not absolute certainty. Identify the three possible positions he mentions.
He lists three options: denial, exaggeration, and the middle path.
3
Understand the pragmatics of belief
Ghazali argues that since belief is unavoidable and only three positions exist, the most plausible one must be adopted, even if doubts cling to it.
The middle position has the least problematic consequences.
4
Reflect on the limitation of theological inquiry
Ghazali acknowledges that complete elimination of doubt is impossible for transcendent topics, but reducing doubt is enough.
This is a methodological humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة: إثباتكم فإن قيل: فليس في هذا قطع دابر الإشكال؛ لأنك إذا اعترفت باختلاف اختلاف المتعلق.. فالإشكال قائم، فما لك وللنظر في سبب للمختلفات لا يرفع الإشكال ما بسبب الاختلاف بعد وجود الاختلاف؟ فأقول: غاية الناصر لمذهب معين أن يظهر على القطع ترجيح اعتقاده على اعتقاد غيره، وقد حصل هذا على القطع؛ إذ لا طريق إلا واحد من هذه الثلاث أو اختراع رابع لا يعقل، وهذا الواحد إذا قوبل بطرفيه المقابلين له.. علم على القطع رجحانه، وإذا لم يكن بد من اعتقاد ولا معتقد إلا هذه الثلاث، وهذا أقرب الثلاث.. فيجب اعتقاده، فيبقى ما يحيك في الصدر من إشكال يلزم على هذا، واللازم على غيره أعظم منه، وتقليل الإشكال ممكن، أما قطعه بالكلية والمنظور فيه هو الصفات القديمة المتعالية عن أفهام الخلق.. فهو أمر ممتنع إلا بتطويل لا يحتمله هذا الكتاب، هذا هو الكلام العام.
Problem: Your affirmation - then if it is said: There is not in this a complete cutting off of the problem; for if you admit the difference of the difference of the relation, then the problem remains. What is it to you and to considering the cause of the different things does not remove the problem? [The problem] exists due to the difference after the existence of the difference? So I say: The utmost help for a particular school is to manifest decisively the preference of his belief over the belief of others, and this has been obtained decisively; for there is no way except one of these three or the invention of a fourth that is not conceivable. And this one, when compared with its two opposing extremes, it is known decisively to be preferable. And since there is no avoiding belief, and there is no object of belief except these three, and this one is the closest of the three, so it is necessary to believe it. Then there remains what rankles in the breast of a problem that attaches to this, and what attaches to others is greater than it. And reducing the problem is possible, but cutting it off entirely—and the object under consideration is the eternal attributes that are elevated above the understandings of creation—that is impossible except with a lengthy exposition that this book cannot bear. This is the general discourse.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer. The objector says that Ghazali's middle position still leaves a problem because even if you allow for differences in relations, the problem of how attributes are distinct remains. Ghazali's answer is pragmatic: the goal of a defender of a school is to show decisively that his belief is preferable to others. Since there are only three possible positions (denial, exaggeration, middle), and the middle compares favorably with both extremes, it must be believed. Residual doubts are inevitable given the subject matter (divine attributes beyond full human comprehension), but the middle position minimizes these doubts. Complete elimination of all doubts is impossible without a much longer treatment.

Key Terms
الإشكال
الترجيح
الاعتقاد
القطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A valid theological argument must remove all doubt entirely.
Correction: Ghazali argues that for transcendent matters, complete removal of doubt is impossible; it is sufficient to show that one position is decisively preferable to alternatives.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's answer, why is the middle position necessary to believe even if doubts remain?
A) Because it is the only position that scripture explicitly states.
B) Because it is the only rationally conceivable position and it is preferable to the other two.
C) Because it removes all possible doubts completely.
D) Because the other positions are forbidden by consensus.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends the middle position by arguing that it is decisively preferable to the alternatives, even if some doubts remain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In matters of faith, do you ever feel paralyzed by doubt or the lack of absolute certainty? How does Ghazali's argument that 'decisive preference' is enough challenge your need for complete certainty? Will you now accept a well-supported belief even if some questions remain?
?
Ghazali admits that the middle position still leaves a problem 'that rankles in the breast.' What is a theological issue that rankles in your own heart? Are you willing to embrace the view that has the most support, even if it doesn't answer every question? Commit to acting on that view today.
?
You cannot avoid belief—whether active or passive, you hold a position on divine attributes. Have you honestly examined which of the three positions (denial, exaggeration, or the middle) you actually hold? If not, what is stopping you from testing your belief against Ghazali's criteria of decisive preference?

Ghazali's Question on the Mu'tazila's Distinction Between Power and Will
Ghazali singles out the Mu'tazila for their distinction between power and will and asks a clarifying question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rhetorical question that challenges the Mu'tazilite distinction between divine power and will.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's distinction between power and will is put to the test by a simple question.

In this passage, Ghazali turns his attention to the Mu'tazila, who famously differentiate between divine power (qudra) and will (irada). He singles them out for this view and poses a pointed question: what exactly is the difference? This sets the stage for a deeper theological critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the brevity and directness of Ghazali's language.
The term 'الاستفراق' may be a corruption of 'الاستفصال' (making a distinction).
2
Identify Ghazali's target
Observe that Ghazali says 'we single them out for making a distinction' – the 'them' refers to the Mu'tazila. This is not a general discussion but a specific sectarian objection.
3
Analyze the question
Focus on the question: 'What difference is there between power and will here?' Consider what 'here' refers to (divine actions). This question exposes a potential logical flaw in the Mu'tazilite position.
The question suggests that the Mu'tazila owe an explanation for why they treat power and will as distinct.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Think about why this question is problematic for the Mu'tazila. If power and will are truly distinct, how do they relate in God's actions? The Ash'ari view, by contrast, often sees will as a subset or aspect of power.
This passage is an opening move; the full argument likely follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما المعتزلة: فإنا نخصهم بالاستفراق بين القدرة والإرادة ونقول: إن أي فرق بين القدرة والإرادة هنا؟
As for the Mu'tazila, we single them out for making a distinction between power and will, and we say: What difference is there between power and will here?
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This is a short passage that introduces a specific point of argument with the Mu'tazila. Ghazali notes that the Mu'tazila are distinctive in that they draw a distinction between power (qudra) and will (irada). He then poses a rhetorical question: what exactly is the difference between power and will in this context? This sets up a forthcoming analysis or refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on the relationship between these two attributes.

Key Terms

How Divine Attributes Are Distinguished: The Role of Relation
Ghazali explains that attribute differences stem from relation variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali argues that the distinction between divine attributes like power and knowledge stems from the fundamental variation in their relational nature, not merely from their objects.

Core Idea
Attribute differences are rooted in the nature of their relation, not just their objects.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a subtle theological issue: how do we distinguish between the various eternal attributes of God, such as power and knowledge? He argues that the difference is not simply a matter of different objects (e.g., knowledge of black vs. white), but rather a deeper variation in the very relation each attribute has to its objects. This principle ensures that the attributes are truly distinct from each other and from the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp Ghazali's flow.
Pay attention to the distinction between two types of difference.
2
Identify the two types of difference
Notice the contrast: difference between power and knowledge vs. difference between knowledge of black and knowledge of white. The former is a difference in the attribute itself; the latter is only in the object.
3
Understand the principle
Ghazali states: any difference that returns to distinctness of essences means one cannot stand in for another. This forces power to be other than knowledge.
This principle applies to all seven attributes and to the essence vs. attributes.
4
Apply the principle to attributes and essence
The disparity between an attribute and the essence is even greater than between two attributes, so attributes are distinct from the essence.
5
Consider the special case of knowledge
Knowledge of different objects differs only by relation, not by essence, so a single eternal knowledge can know multiple objects without multiplicity in the attribute.
This resolves a potential objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف فيهما من جهة تغاير التعلَّقِ ، فليس الاختلاف بين القدرة والعلم كالاختلاف بين العلم بسواد والعلم بسواد آخرَ أو ببياض ؛ ولذلك إذا حددت العلم بحد .. يدخل فيه العلم بالمعلومات كلها فنقول : الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد أن يقال : كلُّ اختلاف يرجع إلى تباين الذوات بأنفسها فلا يمكن أن يكفي الواحد منها وينوب عن المختلفات : فوجب أن يكون العلم غير القدرة ، وكذا الصفات السبع ، وأن تكون الصفاتُ غير الذات من حيث إن المباينة بين الصفة الذات الموصوفة وبين أشدُّ من المباينة بين الصفتين وأما العلم بالشيء.. فلا يخالف العلم بغيره إلا من وجه تعلقِهِ بالمتعلق ، فلا يبعد أن تتميّز الصفةُ القديمة بهذه الخاصية ، وهو ألأ يوجب تباين المتعلقاتِ فيها تبايناً وتعدداً
The difference between them is from the aspect of the variation of relation. So the difference between power and knowledge is not like the difference between knowledge of blackness and knowledge of another blackness or of whiteness. Therefore, if you define knowledge by a definition, it includes knowledge of all objects of knowledge. So we say: The moderation in belief is that it is said: Every difference that returns to the distinctness of the essences themselves, then one cannot suffice for the other and stand in for the different things. So it is necessary that knowledge is other than power, and likewise the seven attributes, and that the attributes are other than the essence, in that the disparity between the attribute and the described essence is greater than the disparity between two attributes. As for knowledge of a thing, it does not differ from knowledge of another except from the aspect of its relation to the related object. So it is not far that the eternal attribute is distinguished by this property, and that it does not necessitate [or 'except that it necessitates'?] the distinctness of the related objects in it as distinctness and multiplicity.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument about the distinction between divine attributes. Ghazali begins by noting that the difference between attributes like power (qudra) and knowledge ('ilm) is not like the difference between two instances of knowledge (e.g., knowledge of black vs. white), which differ only by their objects. Instead, the difference between power and knowledge stems from a variation in the very nature of their relation (ta'alluq). He then states a general principle: any difference that goes back to a distinctness of essences themselves cannot have one attribute suffice for another. Therefore, knowledge must be distinct from power, and likewise the seven attributes are distinct from each other. Furthermore, attributes are distinct from the essence (dhat) because the disparity between an attribute and the essence is greater than that between two attributes. Finally, he clarifies that knowledge of a thing differs from knowledge of another only by its relation to the object, so it is plausible that the eternal attribute is characterized by this property and that it does not necessitate a multiplicity in the attribute itself due to the multiplicity of objects.

Correction: Ghazali emphasizes it is due to the nature of the relation (ta'alluq) itself, not just the objects.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The distinction between divine attributes is grounded in the variation of their relational nature, not merely their objects, ensuring real distinctness while preserving divine simplicity.

Power vs. Will: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazila distinction between power and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God can be powerful without power but not willing without will, and the logical consistency argument used.

Core Idea
If God can be powerful without power, He can be willing without will.

Ghazali engages in a theological debate with the Mu'tazila over divine attributes. They accept that God is powerful by essence without a distinct attribute of power, yet they insist that will requires an attribute. Ghazali exposes the inconsistency in this position using a symmetrical argument. Let's read the passage to see how he dismantles their objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialectical structure: claim, objection, answer.
2
Identify Ghazali's initial argument
Find the sentence where Ghazali states the symmetry between power and will. What is the logical conditional he uses?
He says: if it is permissible to be powerful without power, then it is permissible to be willing without will.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila object that there is a distinction: being powerful by Himself means He is powerful over all possible things, but being willing by Himself would require willing all objects of will, including contradictions, which is impossible.
They argue that will has a unique problem of contradictory objects.
4
Examine Ghazali's counter-argument
Ghazali points out inconsistency: the Mu'tazila themselves limit God's power to some events (excluding actions of animals and generated things), so they accept that being powerful by Himself does not entail power over everything. Similarly, being willing by Himself can be limited to some events.
Thus, the distinction collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي فرق بين القدرة فأما المعتزلة : فإنا نخصُّهم بالاستفراق بين القدرة والإرادة ونقول : إن جاز أن يكون قادراً بغير قدرة .. جاز أن يكون مريداً بغير إرادة ، ولا فُرقان بينهما تحريجة: ثمة فرق بين القدرة والإرادة يجعلنا نمنع القدرة ونثبت الإرادة فإن قيل : هو قادر لنفسه ؛ فلذلك كان قادراً على جميع المقدورات ولو كان مريداً لنفسه .. لكان مريداً لجملة المرادات ، وهو محال ؛ لأن المتضادات يمكن إرادتها على البدل لا على الجمع ، وأما القدرة .. فيجوز أن تتعلق بالضدين ؟ والجواب أن نقول : قولوا : إنه مريد لنفسه ثم يختص ببعض الحادثات . . كما قلتم : قادر لنفسه ولا تتعلَّق قدرته إلا ببعض الحادثات ؛ فإن جملة أفعال الحيوانات والمتولدات خارجةً عن قدرته وإرادته جميعاً عندكم ) ، فإذا جاز ذلك في القدرة . . جاز في الإرادة أيضا
What is the difference between power and will? As for the Mu'tazila, we specifically discuss with them the distinction between power and will, and we say: If it is permissible for Him to be powerful without power, then it is permissible for Him to be willing without will, and there is no distinction between them. Objection: There is a distinction between power and will that makes us deny power and affirm will. For if it is said: He is powerful by Himself; therefore He was powerful over all possible things. And if He were willing by Himself, He would be willing for all objects of will, which is impossible, because contradictory things can be willed on a substitute basis not collectively. As for power, it is permissible for it to be related to opposites. The answer: We say: Say that He is willing by Himself and then specializes to some events, just as you say: He is powerful by Himself and His power is not related except to some events. For the totality of the actions of animals and generated things are outside His power and will according to you. So if that is permissible for power, it is permissible for will also.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a debate with the Mu'tazila on the distinction between power (qudra) and will (irāda). Ghazali first states that if the Mu'tazila allow God to be powerful without an attribute of power (by His essence), then they should also allow Him to be willing without an attribute of will. The Mu'tazila object that there is a distinction: being powerful by Himself means He is powerful over all possible things, but being willing by Himself would require Him to will all objects of will, including contradictions, which is impossible (since contradictory things can only be willed on a substitution basis, not simultaneously). Ghazali answers by pointing out inconsistency: the Mu'tazila themselves limit God's power to some events (excluding actions of animals and generated things), so they accept that being powerful by Himself does not entail power over everything. Similarly, being willing by Himself can be limited to some events. Thus, the distinction collapses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God is powerful by Himself, He must be powerful over everything.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the Mu'tazila themselves limit God's power to some events, so limitation is possible.

Checkpoint

A) That power cannot exist without an attribute
B) That if God were willing by Himself, He would will all things including contradictions
C) That will is a different kind of attribute from power
D) That God's will is eternal but His power is temporal

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves that if the Mu'tazila accept God as powerful without power, they must also accept Him as willing without will, or else be inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Mu'tazila limited God's power to make a point about His will. Where have you unconsciously placed limits on God's power to make your theology more comfortable?
?

First Argument: Absurd Consequence of Denying Real Speech
Ghazali presents a reductio against the philosophers' view of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first argument that if God is described as speaking based on a sound created in another, then He could also be described as sounding or moving, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If speech can be created in another, so can sound and motion—impossible for God.

The philosophers claimed God is speaking (mutakallim) but denied both internal speech and external sounds. They said God creates the perception of sound in the prophet's ear. Ghazali exposes the absurdity of this position: if that suffices for speech, why not for other attributes?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأما الفلاسفة' up to 'وذلك محال' and its translation. Notice how Ghazali builds his reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers affirm that God is mutakallim but deny kalam nafsi and external sounds. They only allow God to create the perception of sound in the prophet's ear.
3
Understand the absurd consequence
Ghazali argues that if it is acceptable for God to be described as speaking due to something occurring in another, then logically God could also be described as sounding (musawwit) or moving due to sounds and motions occurring in others. This is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the philosophers' premise leads to an impossible conclusion, so the premise must be false.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider why this argument is effective: it shows that the philosophers' position violates the principle of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة : فإنهم ناقضوا في الكلام، وهو باطل من وجهين : أحدهما : قولهم : إن الله تعالى متكلم مع أنهم لا يثبتون كلام النفس ولا يثبتون الأصوات في الوجود ، وإنما يثبتون سماع الصوت بالخلْقِ في أُذُنِ النبي من غير صوت من خارج ، فلو جاز أن يكون بما يحدث في دماغ غيره موصوفاً بأنه متكلم .. لجاز أن يكون موصوفاً بأنه مُصوّت ومتحرك بوجود الصوت والحركة في غيره ، وذلك محال . والثاني : أنَّ ما ذكروه ردُّ للشرع كله ؛ فإن ما يدركه النائم خيال لا حقيقة له ، فإذا ردَّ معرفة النبي بكلام الله تعالى إلى التخيل الذي يشبه أضغاث الأحلام.. فلا يثق به النبي ، ولا يكون ذلك علم
As for the philosophers, they contradicted themselves in speech, and it is false from two aspects: First: Their statement that God is speaking (mutakallim) while they do not affirm speech of the soul (kalam nafsi) nor do they affirm sounds in existence. They only affirm the hearing of sound by creation in the ear of the prophet from no external sound. So if it were permissible for Him to be described as speaking by what occurs in the brain of another, then it would be permissible for Him to be described as sounding (musawwit) and moving by the existence of sound and movement in another, and that is impossible. Second: That which they mentioned is a rejection of the entire law (shar‘); for what the sleeper perceives is imagination with no reality. So if the prophet's knowledge of God's speech is reduced to imagination resembling confused dreams, then the prophet would not be certain of it, and it would not be knowledge.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' view on God's speech (kalam) with two arguments. The first argument points out an absurd consequence: the philosophers deny both internal speech (kalam nafsi) and external sounds, claiming that God creates the perception of sound in the prophet's ear without any sound in reality. If this were valid, then God could be described as 'speaking' based on something occurring in another's brain, which would absurdly also allow describing Him as 'sounding' or 'moving' based on sounds and motions in others. The second argument is that this view destroys the certainty of revelation. It likens prophetic experience to a dream or imagination, which lacks reality. If revelation is mere imagination, the prophet cannot be certain, and it would not constitute true knowledge. Thus, the philosophers' position is self-contradictory and harmful to religious law.

Key Terms
متكلم
كلام النفس
أصوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the philosophers' view is a sophisticated explanation of revelation.
Correction: Ghazali shows it leads to an absurdity: if God is called 'speaking' from a perception created in another, He could also be called 'sounding' or 'moving' from similar creations, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence Ghazali draws from the philosophers' position?
A) That God would be reducible to a physical body
B) That God could be described as sounding or moving due to events in others
C) That the prophet would be a mere dreamer
D) That the philosophers are atheists

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first argument shows that the philosophers' view would absurdly allow attributing sound and motion to God, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining God's speech as a physical sound like a voice? This passage shows that such an image actually limits God. What will you do to train your heart to accept that God's speech is beyond any created sound?
?
If you were to believe that God's speech is merely a created perception, what other attributes might you be tempted to reduce? This argument reveals a dangerous slippery slope. Reflect on one area where you might be tempted to limit God's greatness to fit your understanding. What step will you take today to guard against that?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly feel it is the eternal speech of God, not just a created sound? How would your reverence and attention change if you internalized the fact that this Quran is the uncreated attribute of the Creator?

Second Argument: Undermining the Certainty of Revelation
Ghazali argues that the philosophers' view destroys certainty of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second argument that reducing prophetic revelation to imagination destroys its certainty and thus the entire religious law.

Core Idea
Revelation reduced to imagination is no revelation at all.

Beyond the logical absurdity, Ghazali points out a devastating practical consequence: if the prophet's experience of revelation is mere imagination like a dream, he cannot be certain it came from God. This undermines the entire foundation of the law (shar‘).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second argument
Read the Arabic from 'والثاني' to the end, and its translation. Notice how Ghazali compares prophetic experience to dreaming.
2
Identify the philosophers' reduction
The philosophers reduce the prophet's knowledge of God's speech to imagination (takhayyul), which is like confused dreams (adghath ahlam).
3
Understand why this destroys certainty
If the prophet's experience is just imagination, he cannot be certain it is from God. Certainty requires a real source, not subjective fantasy.
4
See the consequence for the law
If prophetic knowledge is not certain, then the entire law (shar‘) based on that knowledge becomes unreliable. Ghazali calls this a rejection of the whole law.
5
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider that this argument is theological: it attacks the reliability of revelation itself, which is central to Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

They only affirm the hearing of sound by creation in the ear of the prophet from no external sound. Resolving an Objection on Divine Power and Will
Ghazali resolves an objection about God's power and will regarding opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes an objection against distinguishing divine power and will by showing that the opponent's own premises allow restriction in both attributes.

Core Idea
Restriction in power implies restriction in will is equally acceptable.

Ghazali engages with an opponent who argues that if God can be powerful without a power attribute, then He should also be willing without a will attribute. The opponent tries to show a contradiction: if God's essence alone were sufficient for will, He would will all things, including opposites simultaneously. Ghazali turns the tables by noting that the opponent already restricts God's power to some events, so restriction in will is no different.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on understanding the opponent's initial argument that links power and will without distinction.
Notice the key terms: قدرة (power), إرادة (will), متضادات (opposites).
2
Trace the opponent's logic
The opponent argues that if God is powerful by His essence, He can will all objects of will. But willing opposites collectively is impossible, so God must have a will attribute. Then he asks why power can relate to opposites while will cannot.
This is a two-part objection: first a parity argument, then a specific difference claim.
3
Understand Ghazali's answer
Ghazali points out that the opponent already accepts that God's power does not extend to all temporal events (e.g., actions of animals). So they accept restriction in power. Similarly, restriction in will is acceptable. Thus the objection fails.
Ghazali uses the opponent's own premises against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جاز أن يكون قادراً بغير قدرة .. جاز أن يكون مريداً بغير إرادة، ولا فرقان بينهما، تحريجة: ثمة فرق فإن قيل: هو قادر لنفسه؛ فلذلك كان قادراً على جميع المقدورات بين القدرة والإرادة ولو كان مريداً لنفسه .. لكان مريداً لجملة المرادات، وهو محال؛ لأن المتضادات يمكن إرادتها على البدل لا على الجمع، وأما القدرة .. فيجوز أن تتعلق بالضدين؟ والجواب أن نقول: قولوا: إنه مريد لنفسه ثم يختص ببعض الحادثات .. كما قلتم: قادر لنفسه ولا تتعلَّق قدرته إلا ببعض الحادثات؛ فإن جملة أفعال الحيوانات والمتولدات خارجةً عن قدرته وإرادته جميعاً عندكم، فإذا جاز ذلك في القدرة .. جاز في الإرادة أيضا
If it is permissible that He is powerful without power, then it is permissible that He is willing without will, and there is no distinction between them. A distinction: there is a difference. For if it is said: He is powerful by Himself; therefore He is powerful over all possibles [while distinguishing between power and will]. And if He were willing by Himself, He would will all objects of will, which is impossible because opposites can be willed alternatively, not collectively. As for power, it is permissible that it relates to both opposites? The answer is to say: you say He is willing by Himself, then He specifies some temporal things, just as you say He is powerful by Himself, yet His power does not relate except to some temporal things. For all the actions of animals and generated things are outside His power and will entirely according to you. If that is permissible in power, then it is permissible in will also.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer. The objector first tries to claim that if God can be powerful without a power attribute, then He should also be willing without a will attribute, with no difference. The author then notes a distinction. The objection proceeds: if God is powerful by His essence, His power extends to all possibles; analogously, if He were willing by His essence, He would will all possible objects of will, including opposites simultaneously, which is impossible. Therefore, He must have a will attribute that restricts willing to alternatives. The objector asks: why can power relate to both opposites while will cannot? The answer: the objectors themselves already admit that God's power does not extend to all temporal events (e.g., human actions and generated things), so they accept restriction in power. Similarly, restriction in will is acceptable. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
متضادات
مقدورات
مرادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think power and will are identical in scope; Ghazali shows they can differ based on the objector's own premises.
Correction: Power and will are distinct attributes with different relations: power can be unrestricted in principle, but both are restricted in actuality according to the opponent's own admission.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why can't God will opposites collectively?
A) Because His will is eternal
B) Because opposites can only be willed on a replacement basis, not collectively
C) Because He has no will
D) Because power and will are the same

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the objection fails because the opponent already accepts restriction in God's power, so restriction in will is not contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make plans, do you truly submit to the possibility that God's will may restrict your desires? What is one plan you are holding too tightly right now, and how can you release it to Allah?
?
If God's power and will are both sovereign yet sometimes restricted in scope according to His wisdom, how does that affect your trust in Him when your own power or will is limited? Can you accept a 'no' from Him as a sign of His perfect plan?
?
The objector assumed that God's will must extend to all things equally, but Ghazali shows that restriction is not a flaw. In your life, where do you struggle with the idea that God's restrictions on you (e.g., tests, delays) are actually expressions of His perfect will? What must change in your heart to embrace this?

The Inconsistency of Philosophers on Divine Speech
First refutation: absurd consequences of philosophers' speech theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first refutation of the philosophers: that denying internal speech and external sounds while calling God 'speaking' leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
You cannot call God 'speaking' if you deny both internal and external speech.

Ghazali turns the philosophers' own claims against them. They assert that God is 'speaking' but reject the reality of internal speech (kalam nafsi) and external sounds. In this passage, Ghazali shows that this position is incoherent: if speech can be attributed to God based on something happening in another being, then other absurd attributes would also follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice Ghazali's two refutations are introduced.
Focus on the first refutation.
2
Identify the philosophers' position
The philosophers claim God speaks but deny internal speech and external sounds. They only allow a sound created in the prophet's ear.
3
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum
Ghazali argues: if speech can be attributed due to something occurring in another, then sound and movement could be attributed similarly, which is impossible.
This shows the philosophers' view is self-contradictory.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that this refutation exposes the logical flaw in their concept of divine speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة : فإنهم ناقضوا في الكلام، وهو باطل من وجهين: أحدهما: قولهم: إن الله تعالى متكلم مع أنهم لا يثبتون كلام النفس ولا يثبتون الأصوات في الوجود، وإنما يثبتون سماع الصوت بالخلْقِ في أُذُنِ النبي من غير صوت من خارج، فلو جاز أن يكون بما يحدث في دماغ غيره موصوفاً بأنه متكلم .. لجاز أن يكون موصوفاً بأنه مُصوّت ومتحرك بوجود الصوت والحركة في غيره، وذلك محال. والثاني: أنَّ ما ذكروه ردُّ للشرع كله؛ فإن ما يدركه النائم خيال لا حقيقة له، فإذا ردَّ معرفة النبي بكلام الله تعالى إلى التخيل الذي يشبه أضغاث الأحلام.. فلا يثق به النبي، ولا يكون ذلك علم
As for the philosophers, they have contradicted themselves regarding speech, and it is false in two ways: First: their statement that God is speaking while they do not affirm internal speech, nor do they affirm sounds in existence; they only affirm hearing of sound by creation in the ear of the prophet without any sound from outside. If it were permissible that one is described as speaking due to what occurs in the brain of another, then it would be permissible that he be described as sounding and moving by the existence of sound and movement in another, and that is impossible. Second: what they have mentioned is a rejection of the entire Law; for what the sleeper perceives is imagination with no reality. If the prophet's knowledge of God's speech is reduced to imagination resembling confused dreams, then the prophet would not be certain of it, and that would not be knowledge.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' theory of divine speech and revelation. Ghazali gives two arguments. First, the philosophers claim God is 'speaking' but deny both internal speech (kalam nafsi) and external sounds; they only allow that God creates a sound in the prophet's ear without an external source. Ghazali argues that if being described as 'speaking' is based on something happening in someone else's brain, then by the same logic God could be described as 'sounding' or 'moving' based on sounds or movements in others, which is absurd. Second, the philosophers reduce prophetic experience to mere imagination like dreams; this undermines the certainty of revelation. If the prophet cannot distinguish revelation from a dream, he has no real knowledge, and the entire Sharia is rejected.

Key Terms
كلام
كلام النفس
أصوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers believe in a meaningful concept of divine speech.
Correction: Ghazali shows they deny both internal speech and external sounds, reducing speech to an event in the hearer, which is not true speech.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say would be the consequence if speech is attributed due to something happening in another?
A) God would be described as sounding and moving
B) God would be silent
C) The prophet would be the speaker
D) Speech would be eternal

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first refutation shows that the philosophers' view on divine speech leads to absurdity because it would allow attributing sound and movement to God based on events in others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ghazali's argument exposes the logical absurdity of attributing speech to God based on something happening in another. Are there areas in your own faith where you hold beliefs that logically lead to contradictions? What will you do to correct them?
?
The philosophers' error stemmed from denying a real attribute of God. In your daily life, do you ever downplay or spiritualize away God's attributes to fit your own comfort? How can you ensure that your theology honors God as He truly is?

Prophecy and Certainty: Ghazali's Refutation of the Dream Theory
Second refutation: reducing revelation to dreams destroys certainty and the Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second refutation: that reducing prophetic revelation to imagination like dreams undermines the certainty of knowledge and the entire Sharia.

Core Idea
If revelation is just a dream, the prophet can never be certain.

The philosophers not only distort divine speech but also undermine the very foundation of prophethood. Ghazali argues that equating revelation with dream imagery destroys the epistemic security of the prophet. If the prophet cannot distinguish between true revelation and a mere dream, he has no certainty, and the entire religious law collapses.

3

The prophet would not have certainty because he can't distinguish revelation from imagination.
4
Reflect on the wider impact
This would reject the entire Sharia, as argued by Ghazali.
